Parsing [sent. 1700 len. 54]: The property which the old gentleman had bequeathed to my mother was added to that which my father had already vested in the brig , and he then owned one-third of the vessel ; the other two-thirds were the property of a very rich ship-builder and owner , of the name of Masterman .
Parsing [sent. 1701 len. 25]: What with the profits of the share he held of the vessel and his pay as captain , my father was well to do .
Parsing [sent. 1702 len. 48]: Mr. Masterman , who had a very high opinion of my father , and gained much money by his exertions and good management , was present at the marriage , and when I was born , about a year afterwards , he stood for me as godfather .
Parsing [sent. 1703 len. 37]: Every one considered that this was a most advantageous circumstance for me , and congratulated my father and mother ; for Mr. Masterman was a bachelor , of nearly sixty years , without any near relations .
Parsing [sent. 1704 len. 36]: It is true , that he was very fond of money ; but that , they said , was all the better , as he could not take it away with him when he died .
Parsing [sent. 1705 len. 67]: An end , however , was soon put to all their worldly ideas , for a year after I was born , my father was drowned at sea , his vessel and the whole of her crew being lost on the Texel sands ; and my mother found herself a widow , with a child scarcely weaned , when she was but twenty-two years of age .
Parsing [sent. 1706 len. 56]: `` It was supposed that my mother would still have sufficient to live upon , as the ship had been insured at two-thirds of her value ; but , to the astonishment of everybody , Mr. Masterman contrived to make it appear that it was his two-thirds of the vessel which had been insured . ''
Parsing [sent. 1707 len. 6]: `` What is insurance ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1708 len. 3]: inquired William .
Parsing [sent. 1709 len. 47]: `` Insurance , my dear boy , is paying a certain sum to people who are called underwriters , that in case the vessel or cargo is lost or damaged , the loss or damage is made good to the owners of the vessel or cargo .
Parsing [sent. 1710 len. 9]: You pay in proportion to the risk incurred .
Parsing [sent. 1711 len. 67]: Supposing you wished to insure one thousand pounds on a vessel or cargo , and ten per cent was required , you would , if the vessel came home safe , pay the underwriters one hundred pounds ; if , on the contrary , the vessel was lost , the underwriters would have to pay you one thousand pounds , the sum which you had insured .
Parsing [sent. 1712 len. 11]: I beg your pardon for the interruption , Ready . ''
Parsing [sent. 1713 len. 18]: `` No need , Mr. Seagrave ; we never should lose an opportunity of teaching the young .
Parsing [sent. 1714 len. 81]: Well , how far the assertion of Mr. Masterman was correct or not , it was impossible at the time to say ; but I do know that everybody cried out ` shame ' , and that if he did deprive the widow , he had much to answer for ; for the Bible says , ` Pure religion is to visit the fatherless and the widows in their affliction , and to keep yourself unspotted in the world ' .
Parsing [sent. 1715 len. 45]: The consequence was , that my mother had little or nothing to live upon ; but she found friends who assisted her , and she worked embroidery , and contrived to get on somehow until I was eight or nine years of age . ''
Parsing [sent. 1716 len. 16]: `` But did not your godfather come forward to the assistance of your mother ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1717 len. 4]: inquired Mr. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 1718 len. 21]: `` No , sir , strange to say , he did not ; and that made people talk the more .
Parsing [sent. 1719 len. 49]: I believe it was the abuse of him , which he did not fail to hear , and which he ascribed to my mother , which turned him away from us ; perhaps it was his own conscience , for we always dislike those we have injured . ''
Parsing [sent. 1720 len. 34]: `` Unfortunately , there is great truth in that remark of yours , Ready , '' observed Mr. Seagrave ; `` still , it is strange that he did not do something . ''
Parsing [sent. 1721 len. 40]: `` It was very strange , sir , - at least , so it appeared at the time , but he was very fond of money , and irritated at the reports and observations which were made about him .
Parsing [sent. 1722 len. 41]: But , to go on , sir , I was a strong , hardy boy , and , whenever I could escape from my mother or school , was always found by the water-side or on board of the vessels .
Parsing [sent. 1723 len. 18]: In the summer-time I was half the day in water , and was a very good swimmer .
Parsing [sent. 1724 len. 22]: My mother perceived my fondness for the profession , and tried all she could to divert my thoughts some other way .
Parsing [sent. 1725 len. 27]: She told me of the dangers and hardships which sailors went through , and always ended with my father 's death and a flood of tears .
Parsing [sent. 1726 len. 38]: `` We certainly are of a perverse nature , as I have often heard the clergyman say , for it appears to me that we always wish to do that which we are told not to do .
Parsing [sent. 1727 len. 24]: If my mother had not been always persuading me against going to sea , I really believe I might have stayed at home .
Parsing [sent. 1728 len. 15]: I 've often thought since , how selfish and unfeeling I must have been .
Parsing [sent. 1729 len. 27]: I was too young to know what pain I was giving my mother , and how anxiety was preying upon her , all on my account .
Parsing [sent. 1730 len. 27]: Children can not feel it ; if they did , they would do otherwise , for our hearts are seldom hard until we grow older . ''
Parsing [sent. 1731 len. 13]: `` I agree with you , Ready , '' said Mr. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 1732 len. 34]: `` If children really knew how much their parents suffer when they behave ill , how alarmed they are at any proofs of wickedness in them , they would be much better . ''
Parsing [sent. 1733 len. 19]: `` We never find that out , sir , till it is too late , '' continued Ready .
Parsing [sent. 1734 len. 77]: `` Well , sir , I was little more than nine years old , when , on a very windy day , and the water rough , a hawser , by which a vessel was fast to the wharf , was carried away with a violent jerk , and the broken part , as it flew out , struck a person who was at the edge of the wharf , and knocked him into the sea .
Parsing [sent. 1735 len. 45]: I heard the crying out , and the men from the wharf and from the ships were throwing ropes to him , but he could not catch hold of them ; indeed , he could not swim well , and the water was rough .
Parsing [sent. 1736 len. 17]: I caught a rope that had been hauled in again , and leapt off the wharf .
Parsing [sent. 1737 len. 26]: `` Young as I was , I swam like a duck , and put the rope into his hands just as he was going down .
Parsing [sent. 1738 len. 42]: He clung to it as drowning men only can cling , and was hauled to the piles , and soon afterwards a boat , which had been lowered from the stern of one of the vessels , picked us both up .
Parsing [sent. 1739 len. 37]: We were taken to a public-house , and put into bed till dry clothes could be sent for us ; and then I found that the person I had saved was my godfather , Mr. Masterman .
Parsing [sent. 1740 len. 32]: Everyone was loud in my praise ; and , although perhaps I ought not to say it , it was a bold act for so young a boy as I was .
Parsing [sent. 1741 len. 73]: The sailors took me home to my mother in a sort of triumphal procession ; and she , poor thing , when she heard what I had done , embraced me over and over again , one moment rejoicing at my preservation , and the next weeping bitterly at the thoughts of the danger I had encountered , and the probability that my bold spirit would lead me into still greater . ''
Parsing [sent. 1742 len. 14]: `` But she did not blame you for what you had done ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1743 len. 41]: `` Oh no , William ; she felt that I had done my duty towards my neighbour , and perhaps she felt in her own heart that I had returned good for evil ; but she did not say so .
Parsing [sent. 1744 len. 30]: The next day Mr. Masterman called upon us ; he certainly looked very foolish and confused when he asked for his godson , whom he had so long neglected .
Parsing [sent. 1745 len. 62]: My mother , who felt how useful he might be to me , received him very kindly ; but I had been often told of his neglect of me and my mother , and of his supposed unfair conduct towards my father , and had taken a violent dislike to him ; his advances towards me were therefore very coolly received .
Parsing [sent. 1746 len. 76]: I felt glad that I had saved him ; but although I could not exactly understand my own feelings at the time , I am ashamed to say that my pleasure was not derived from having done a good action , so much as indulging a feeling of revenge in having put one under an obligation who had treated me ill ; this arose from my proud spirit , which my mother could not check .
Parsing [sent. 1747 len. 37]: So you see , William , there was very little merit in what I had done , as , after I had done it , I indulged those feelings which I ought to have checked . ''
Parsing [sent. 1748 len. 22]: `` I think I could not have helped feeling the same , Ready , under such circumstances , '' replied William .
Parsing [sent. 1749 len. 33]: `` The impulse which induced me to act was good , '' replied Ready ; `` but the feeling which I indulged in afterwards took away the whole merit of the deed .
Parsing [sent. 1750 len. 30]: I am stating what I believe to be the truth ; and an old man like me can look upon the past without bias , but not without regret .
Parsing [sent. 1751 len. 53]: Mr. Masterman made but a short visit ; he told my mother that he would now take care of me and bring me up to the business of a ship-builder as soon as I was old enough to leave school , and that in the meantime he would pay all my expenses .
Parsing [sent. 1752 len. 46]: My poor mother was very grateful , and shed tears of joy ; and when Mr. Masterman went away , she embraced me , and said that now she was happy , as I should have a profession on shore and not go to sea .
Parsing [sent. 1753 len. 57]: I must do justice to Mr. Masterman ; he kept his word and sent money to my mother , so that she became quite cheerful and comfortable , and everyone congratulated her , and she used to fondle me , and say , it was all through me that she was relieved from her distress . ''
Parsing [sent. 1754 len. 12]: `` How happy that must have made you , Ready ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1755 len. 3]: said William .
Parsing [sent. 1756 len. 14]: `` Yes , it did , but it made me also very proud .
Parsing [sent. 1757 len. 22]: Strange to say , I could not conquer my dislike to Mr. Masterman ; I had nourished the feeling too long .
Parsing [sent. 1758 len. 46]: I could not bear that my mother should be under obligations to him , or that he should pay for my schooling ; it hurt my foolish pride , young as I then was ; and although my mother was happy , I was not .
Parsing [sent. 1759 len. 40]: Besides , as I was put to a better school , and was obliged to remain with the other boys , I could no longer run about the wharfs , or go on board the vessels , as before .
Parsing [sent. 1760 len. 46]: I did not see then , as I do now , that it was all for my good but I became discontented and unhappy , merely because I was obliged to pay attention to my learning , and could no longer have my own way .
Parsing [sent. 1761 len. 15]: The master complained of me ; and Mr. Masterman called and scolded me well .
Parsing [sent. 1762 len. 11]: I became more disobedient , and then I was punished .
Parsing [sent. 1763 len. 18]: This irritated me , and I made up my mind that I would run away to sea .
Parsing [sent. 1764 len. 44]: You see , William , I was all in the wrong ; and so will all boys be who think they know better than those who have charge of them ; and now only see what I probably lost by my foolish conduct .
Parsing [sent. 1765 len. 117]: I say <i> probably </i> , for no one can calculate or foresee what is to take place ; but , as far as appearances went , I had every prospect of receiving a good education - of succeeding Mr. Masterman in his business , and , very probably , of inheriting his large fortune ; so that I might have been at this time a rich and well-educated man , surrounded with all the comforts and luxuries of life ; perhaps with an amiable wife and large family round me , to make me still happier , instead of being what I now am , a poor , worn-out old seaman upon a desert isle .
Parsing [sent. 1766 len. 63]: I point this out to you , William , to show how one false and foolish step in the young may affect their whole prospects in life ; and , instead of enabling them to sail down with the stream of prosperity , may leave them to struggle against the current of adversity , as has been the case with me . ''
Parsing [sent. 1767 len. 17]: `` It is , indeed , a good lesson , Ready , '' said Mr. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 1768 len. 23]: `` It is ; not that I repine at my lot , even while I regret the errors that led to it .
Parsing [sent. 1769 len. 34]: An all-wise and gracious God disposes of us as he thinks best ; and I can now say with perfect sincerity , ` Thy will , not mine , be done ' . ''
Parsing [sent. 1770 len. 50]: `` Your misfortunes have , however , proved an incalculable benefit to us , Ready , '' observed Mrs. Seagrave ; `` for had you not gone to sea , and been on board the ship when the crew deserted us , what would have become of us ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1771 len. 25]: `` Well , madam , it is some comfort to think that a worn-out old seaman like myself has been of some use . ''
Parsing [sent. 1772 len. 16]: Chapter XXXII The bleating of the kids woke them the next morning earlier than usual .
Parsing [sent. 1773 len. 21]: The weather was again fine , and the sun shining brightly , and Ready turned out Nanny and her progeny .
Parsing [sent. 1774 len. 67]: They had an excellent breakfast of fried fish , and then Mr. Seagrave , Ready , and William went out to their work : the two first took down the tents , and spread the canvas on the ground , that it might be well dried , while William went in pursuit of the fowls , which had not been seen for a day or two .
Parsing [sent. 1775 len. 22]: After half-an-hour 's search in the cocoa-nut grove , he heard the cock crow , and soon afterwards found them all .
Parsing [sent. 1776 len. 14]: He threw them some split peas , which he had brought with him .
Parsing [sent. 1777 len. 25]: They were hungry enough and followed him home to the house , where he left them and went to join Ready and his father .
Parsing [sent. 1778 len. 59]: `` William , '' said Ready , `` I think , now that we have spread out the tents , we will , if Mr. Seagrave approves , all set to at once and knock up a fowl-house ; it wo n't be more than a day 's job , and then the creatures will have a home .
Parsing [sent. 1779 len. 28]: There are four very thick cocoa-nut trees close to the house ; we will build it under them ; it will be a good job over . ''
Parsing [sent. 1780 len. 11]: Mr. Seagrave assented , and they set immediately to work .
Parsing [sent. 1781 len. 52]: There were many thin poles left , the tops of the cocoa-nut trees which had been cut down to build the house ; these they nailed to the trunks of the four trees , so as to make a square , and then they ran up rafters for a pitched roof .
Parsing [sent. 1782 len. 55]: `` Now , sir , this is only rough work ; we will first put up a perch or two for them , and then close in the side , and thatch the roof with cocoa-nut branches ; but there 's Juno taking in the dinner , so we 'll finish it afterwards . ''
Parsing [sent. 1783 len. 36]: After their meal the work was renewed ; Mr. Seagrave collected the branches while William and Ready worked upon the sides and roof , and before the evening closed in , the fowl-house was complete .
Parsing [sent. 1784 len. 18]: William enticed the fowls down to it with some more split peas , and then walked away .
Parsing [sent. 1785 len. 34]: `` Now , sir , the creatures will soon find their way in ; and by and by , when I have time , I 'll make a door to the entrance . ''
Parsing [sent. 1786 len. 38]: `` And now , '' said William , `` I think we had better roll up the canvas of the tents ; we have had a splendid day , and may not be so fortunate to-morrow . ''
Parsing [sent. 1787 len. 25]: `` Very true ; we will get them housed , and stow them away under the bed-places ; there is plenty of room . ''
Parsing [sent. 1788 len. 33]: By the time that they had folded up the canvas , and William had brought in Nanny and the kids , the sun had set , and they went into the house .
Parsing [sent. 1789 len. 46]: Ready was requested to go on with his history , which he did as follows : -- `` I said last night that I determined to run away from school and go to sea , but I did not tell you how I managed it .
Parsing [sent. 1790 len. 22]: I had no chance of getting out of the school unperceived , except after the boys were all put to bed .
Parsing [sent. 1791 len. 61]: The room that I slept in was at the top of the house - the doors I knew were all locked ; but there was a trap-door which led out on the roof , fastened by a bolt inside , and a ladder leading up to it ; and I determined that I would make my escape by that way .
Parsing [sent. 1792 len. 25]: As soon as all the other boys were fast asleep , I arose and dressed myself very quietly , and then left the room .
Parsing [sent. 1793 len. 21]: `` The moon shone bright , which was lucky for me , and I gained the trap-door without any noise .
Parsing [sent. 1794 len. 37]: I had some difficulty in forcing it up , as it was heavy for a boy of my age ; but I contrived to do so at last , and gained the roof of the house .
Parsing [sent. 1795 len. 58]: I then began looking about me , to see how I was to get to the ground , and after walking to and fro several times , I decided that I could slip down by a large water-pipe ; it was so far detached from the bricks , that I could get my small fingers round it .
Parsing [sent. 1796 len. 32]: I climbed over the parapet , and , clinging to the pipe firmly with my hands and knees , I slid down , and arrived at the bottom in safety . ''
Parsing [sent. 1797 len. 19]: `` It 's a wonder you did not break your neck , Ready , '' observed Mrs. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 1798 len. 8]: `` It was , indeed , ma'am .
Parsing [sent. 1799 len. 38]: As soon as I was landed in the flowerbed , which was below , I hastened to the iron gates at the entrance , and soon climbed up and got to the other side into the road .
Parsing [sent. 1800 len. 133]: I started as fast as I could towards the port , and when I arrived at the wharf , I perceived that a vessel had her topsails loose , and meant to take advantage of the ebb-tide which had just made ; the men were singing ` Yo heave yo ' , getting the anchor up ; and as I stood watching , almost making up my mind that I would swim off to her , I perceived that a man pushed off in her jolly-boat , and was sculling to a post a little higher up , where a hawser had been made fast ; I ran round , and arrived there before he had cast off the rope ; without saying a word , I jumped into the boat . ''
Parsing [sent. 1801 len. 9]: ` What do you want , youngster ? '
Parsing [sent. 1802 len. 5]: said the seaman . ''
Parsing [sent. 1803 len. 25]: ` I want to go to sea , ' said I , breathless ; ` take me on board - pray do . ' ''
Parsing [sent. 1804 len. 25]: ` Well , ' said he , ` I heard the captain say he wanted an apprentice , and so you may come . '
Parsing [sent. 1805 len. 19]: `` He sculled the boat back again to the vessel , and I climbed up her side . ''
Parsing [sent. 1806 len. 6]: ` Who are you ? '
Parsing [sent. 1807 len. 4]: said the captain .
Parsing [sent. 1808 len. 13]: `` I told him that I wanted to go to sea . ''
Parsing [sent. 1809 len. 11]: ` You are too little and too young . ' ''
Parsing [sent. 1810 len. 25]: ` No , I am not , ' replied I. '' ` Why , do you think that you dare go aloft ? ' ''
Parsing [sent. 1811 len. 28]: ` I 'll show you , ' replied I ; and I ran up the rigging like a cat , and went out at the topgallant yard-arm .
Parsing [sent. 1812 len. 48]: `` When I came down , the captain said , ` Well , I think you 'll make a sharp seaman by and by ; so I 'll take you , and , as soon as I get to London , I 'll bind you apprentice . '
Parsing [sent. 1813 len. 37]: `` The ship , which was a collier , was soon out of port , and before the day had dawned I found myself on the wide ocean , which was hereafter to be my home .
Parsing [sent. 1814 len. 99]: `` As soon as the hurry and confusion were over , I was examined by the captain , who appeared to me to be a very rough , harsh man ; indeed , before the day was over I almost repented of the step which I had taken , and when I sat down cold and wet upon some old sail at night , the thoughts of my mother , and what distress I should occasion her , for the first time rushed into my mind , and I wept bitterly ; but it was too late then .
Parsing [sent. 1815 len. 40]: I have often thought , Mr. Seagrave , that the life of hardship which I have since gone through has been a judgment on me for my cruelty to my mother , in leaving her the way I did .
Parsing [sent. 1816 len. 18]: It broke her heart ; a poor return , William , for all her care and kindness !
Parsing [sent. 1817 len. 5]: God forgive me ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1818 len. 18]: Old Ready left off for some little time , and the remainder of the party kept silence .
Parsing [sent. 1819 len. 40]: Then he said - `` I 'll leave off now , if you please : I do n't feel inclined to go on ; my heart is full when I recall that foolish and wicked deed of mine . ''
Parsing [sent. 1820 len. 71]: Chapter XXXIII The next morning was fine , and as soon as breakfast was over , they took the wheels down to the turtle-pond , and Ready having speared one of the largest by means of a pike with a barb to it , which he had made on purpose , they hauled it on shore , slung it under the wheels , and took it up to the house .
Parsing [sent. 1821 len. 89]: Having killed the turtle , and cut it up , Juno , under the directions of Ready , chose such portions as were required for the soup ; and when the pot was on the fire , Ready , Mr. Seagrave , and William set off with the cross-cut saw and hatchets , to commence felling the cocoa-nut trees for the building of the outhouse , which was to hold their stores , as soon as they could be brought round from the other side of the island .
Parsing [sent. 1822 len. 103]: `` I mean this to be our place of refuge in case of danger , sir , '' observed Ready ; `` and therefore I have selected this thick part of the wood , as it is not very far from the house , and by cutting the path to it in a zigzag , it will be quite hidden from sight ; and we must make the path just wide enough to allow the wheels to pass , and stump up the roots of the trees which we are obliged to cut down , otherwise the stumps would attract attention . ''
Parsing [sent. 1823 len. 23]: `` I agree with you , Ready , '' replied Mr. Seagrave ; `` there is no saying what may happen . ''
Parsing [sent. 1824 len. 38]: `` You see , sir , it is often the custom for the natives , in this part of the world , to come in their canoes from one island to another , merely to get cocoa-nuts .
Parsing [sent. 1825 len. 33]: I ca n't say that the other islands near us are inhabited , but still it is probable , and we can not tell what the character of the people may be .
Parsing [sent. 1826 len. 24]: I tell you this , but we had better not say a word to Mrs. Seagrave , as it may distress her . ''
Parsing [sent. 1827 len. 18]: Mr. Seagrave agreed , and Ready continued : `` We are now near the spot , sir .
Parsing [sent. 1828 len. 33]: You see , when we have got over this hill , where the trees are so very thick , the fall in the ground will assist in the concealment of the building .
Parsing [sent. 1829 len. 14]: I should say we are very near right where we now stand . ''
Parsing [sent. 1830 len. 10]: `` How far are we now from the house ?
Parsing [sent. 1831 len. 8]: We must not be too distant . ''
Parsing [sent. 1832 len. 29]: `` I reckon we are not 150 yards in a straight line , although the road will , by its turning , make it double the distance . ''
Parsing [sent. 1833 len. 12]: `` Then I think this spot will do very well . ''
Parsing [sent. 1834 len. 37]: `` I 'll just mark out the trees which are to stand , Mr. Seagrave , and those which are to be cut down , so as to leave about four feet of stump standing . ''
Parsing [sent. 1835 len. 28]: As soon as they had planned the building , the axes and saw were in full use , and tree after tree fell one upon the other .
Parsing [sent. 1836 len. 23]: They worked hard till dinner-time , and were not sorry at the prospect of sitting down to a rich mess of turtle-soup .
Parsing [sent. 1837 len. 19]: `` My dear William , and you too , Mr. Seagrave , how very warm you are ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1838 len. 13]: said Mrs. Seagrave ; `` you must not work so hard . ''
Parsing [sent. 1839 len. 32]: `` Cutting down trees is very warm work , mother , '' replied William , `` and hard work will never hurt any one , especially when he dines off turtle-soup .
Parsing [sent. 1840 len. 12]: Why , Tommy , what 's the matter with you ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1841 len. 13]: `` Tommy and I are at variance , '' replied Mrs. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 1842 len. 36]: `` I had my thimble this morning , and had commenced my sewing , when I was called out by Juno , and Caroline went with me , and Tommy was left in the house .
Parsing [sent. 1843 len. 45]: When I came back I found him outside , and on going back to my work , there was no thimble to be found ; I asked him if he had touched it , and his answer was that he would look for it .
Parsing [sent. 1844 len. 39]: He did look , and said he could not find it ; I have asked him several times if he took it away , and his only answer is that he will find it by and by . ''
Parsing [sent. 1845 len. 10]: `` Tommy , did you take the thimble ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1846 len. 6]: said Mr. Seagrave , gravely .
Parsing [sent. 1847 len. 12]: `` I 'll find it by and by , papa . ''
Parsing [sent. 1848 len. 7]: `` That 's not an answer .
Parsing [sent. 1849 len. 7]: Did you take the thimble ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1850 len. 17]: `` I 'll find it by and by , papa , '' said Tommy , whimpering .
Parsing [sent. 1851 len. 16]: `` That 's all the answer he will give me , '' said Mrs. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 1852 len. 22]: `` Well , then , he shall have no dinner till the thimble makes its appearance , '' replied Mr. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 1853 len. 9]: Master Tommy began to cry at this intelligence .
Parsing [sent. 1854 len. 20]: Juno appeared with the turtle-soup ; and Tommy cried louder when they had said grace and commenced their dinner .
Parsing [sent. 1855 len. 34]: They were all very hungry , and William sent his plate for another portion , which he had not commenced long before he put his finger in his mouth and pulled out something .
Parsing [sent. 1856 len. 17]: `` Why , mother , here 's the thimble in my soup , '' cried William .
Parsing [sent. 1857 len. 41]: `` No wonder he said he would find it by and by , '' said Ready , smiling ; `` he meant to have fished it up , I suppose , from what was left of the soup after dinner .
Parsing [sent. 1858 len. 41]: Well , Mrs. Seagrave , I do n't mean to say that Tommy is a good boy , but still , although be would not tell where the thimble was , he has not told a falsehood about it . ''
Parsing [sent. 1859 len. 11]: `` No , he has not , '' replied William .
Parsing [sent. 1860 len. 22]: `` I think , now that the thimble is found , if he begs pardon , papa will forgive him . ''
Parsing [sent. 1861 len. 11]: `` Tommy , come here , '' said Mr. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 1862 len. 13]: `` Tell me why you put that thimble into the soup ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1863 len. 8]: `` I wanted to taste the soup .
Parsing [sent. 1864 len. 22]: I wanted to fill the thimble ; the soup burnt my fingers , and I let the thimble drop in . ''
Parsing [sent. 1865 len. 17]: `` Well , a thimbleful was n't much , at all events , '' observed Ready .
Parsing [sent. 1866 len. 15]: `` And why did n't you tell your mamma where the thimble was ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1867 len. 20]: `` I was afraid mamma throw all the soup away , and then I get none for dinner . ''
Parsing [sent. 1868 len. 3]: `` Oh !
Parsing [sent. 1869 len. 7]: that was it , was it ?
Parsing [sent. 1870 len. 49]: Well , sir , I said you should have no dinner till the thimble was found , so , as it is found , you may have your dinner ; but if you ever refuse to answer a question again , I shall punish you more severely . ''
Parsing [sent. 1871 len. 49]: Tommy was glad the lecture was over , and more glad to get his turtle-soup ; he finished one plate , and , as he asked for another , he said , `` Tommy wo n't put thimble in again ; put tin pot in next time . ''
Parsing [sent. 1872 len. 18]: After dinner they went to their work again , and did not come in again till sunset .
Parsing [sent. 1873 len. 21]: `` The clouds are gathering fast , sir , '' observed Ready ; `` we shall have rain to-night . ''
Parsing [sent. 1874 len. 16]: `` I fear we shall ; but we must expect it now , Ready . ''
Parsing [sent. 1875 len. 18]: `` Yes , sir ; and by and by we shall have it for days together . ''
Parsing [sent. 1876 len. 26]: `` Ready , '' said Mrs. Seagrave , `` if you are not too tired , perhaps you will go on with your history . ''
Parsing [sent. 1877 len. 14]: `` Certainly , ma'am , if you wish it , '' replied Ready .
Parsing [sent. 1878 len. 18]: `` When I left off , I was on board of the collier , bound to London .
Parsing [sent. 1879 len. 12]: We had a very fair wind , and a quick passage .
Parsing [sent. 1880 len. 44]: I was very sick until we arrived in the Nore , and then I recovered , and , as you may suppose , was astonished at the busy scene , and the quantity of vessels which were going up and down the river .
Parsing [sent. 1881 len. 65]: But I did not like my captain ; he was very severe and brutal to the men ; and the apprentice who was on board told me to run away , and get into another vessel , and not to bind myself apprentice to this captain , or I should be beat all day long , and be treated as bad as he was .
Parsing [sent. 1882 len. 19]: I knew this was the case , as the captain kicked and cuffed him twenty times a day .
Parsing [sent. 1883 len. 42]: The men said that he did not do so to me , for fear I should refuse to be his apprentice ; but that , as soon as my indentures were signed , he would treat me in the same way .
Parsing [sent. 1884 len. 37]: `` Well , I made up my mind that I would not remain in the collier ; and , as the captain had gone on shore , I had plenty of time to look about me .
Parsing [sent. 1885 len. 53]: There was a large ship , which was ready to sail , lying in the stream ; I spoke to two boys who were at the stairs in her boat , and they told me that they were very comfortable on board , and that the captain wanted two or three apprentices .
Parsing [sent. 1886 len. 11]: I went on board with them , and offered myself .
Parsing [sent. 1887 len. 28]: The captain asked me a great many questions , and I told him the truth , and why I did not like to remain in the collier .
Parsing [sent. 1888 len. 42]: He agreed to take me ; and I went on shore with him , signed my indentures , and received from him a sufficient supply of clothes ; and , two days afterwards , we sailed for Bombay and China . ''
Parsing [sent. 1889 len. 15]: `` But you wrote to your mother , Ready , did you not ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1890 len. 3]: said William .
Parsing [sent. 1891 len. 47]: `` Yes , I did ; for the captain desired me to do so , and he put a few lines at the bottom to comfort her ; but , unfortunately , the letter , which was sent on shore by the cook , never arrived .
Parsing [sent. 1892 len. 42]: Whether he dropped it , or forgot it till after the ship sailed , and then tore it up , I do not know ; but , as I found out afterwards , it never did get to her hands . ''
Parsing [sent. 1893 len. 19]: `` It was not your fault that the letter did not arrive safe , '' said Mrs. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 1894 len. 19]: `` No , madam , that was not my fault ; the fault had been committed before . ''
Parsing [sent. 1895 len. 30]: `` Do n't dwell any more upon that portion of your history , Ready ; but tell us what took place after you sailed for the East Indies . ''
Parsing [sent. 1896 len. 9]: `` Be it so , if you please .
Parsing [sent. 1897 len. 34]: I certainly was very smart and active for my age , and soon became a great favourite on board , especially with the lady passengers , because I was such a little fellow .
Parsing [sent. 1898 len. 26]: We arrived safely at Bombay , where our passengers went on shore , and in three weeks afterwards we sailed down the straits for China .
Parsing [sent. 1899 len. 52]: It was war time , and we were very often chased by French privateers ; but as we had a good crew and plenty of guns , none of them ventured to attack us , and we got safe to Macao , where we unloaded our cargo and took in teas .
Parsing [sent. 1900 len. 16]: We had to wait some time for a convoy , and then sailed for England .
Parsing [sent. 1901 len. 46]: When we were off the Isle of France , the convoy was dispersed in a gale ; and three days afterwards , a French frigate bore down upon us , and after exchanging a few broadsides , we were compelled to haul down our colours .
Parsing [sent. 1902 len. 25]: A lieutenant was sent on board with forty men to take charge of us , for we were a very rich prize to them .
Parsing [sent. 1903 len. 50]: The captain and most of the crew were taken on board of the frigate , but ten Lascars and the boys were left in the Indiaman , to assist in taking her into the Isle of France , which was at that time in the hands of the French .
Parsing [sent. 1904 len. 37]: I thought it hard that I was to go to prison at twelve years old ; but I did not care much about it , and very soon I was as gay and merry as ever .
Parsing [sent. 1905 len. 104]: We had made the island , and were on a wind beating up to the port , when a vessel was seen to windward , and although I could not understand what the Frenchmen said , I perceived that they were in a great fluster and very busy with their spy-glasses , and Jack Romer , one of my brother ` prentices who had been three years at sea , said to me , ` I do n't think we 'll go to prison after all , Ready , for that vessel is an English man-of-war , if I 'm not mistaken . '
Parsing [sent. 1906 len. 20]: At last she came down within three miles of us , and hoisted English colours and fired a gun .
Parsing [sent. 1907 len. 115]: The Frenchmen put the ship before the wind , but it was of no use ; the man-of-war came up with us very fast , and then the Frenchmen began to pack up their clothes , together with all the other things which they had collected out of the property of our captain and crew ; a shot was fired which went clean over our heads , and then they left the helm , and Jack Romer went to it , and , with my help , hove the ship up in the wind ; a boat came on board and took possession , and so there was one escape , at all events .
Parsing [sent. 1908 len. 58]: `` They sent a midshipman as prize-master on board of the vessel , and left all us , who had been taken prisoners by the French , in the vessel , to help to work her into port , as the captain did not wish to part with any more men of his own than was necessary .
Parsing [sent. 1909 len. 31]: We soon made sail for England , quite delighted at having escaped a French prison , but , after all , we only exchanged it for a Dutch one . ''
Parsing [sent. 1910 len. 7]: `` How do you mean ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1911 len. 28]: `` I mean that , two days afterwards , as we were rounding the Cape , another French vessel bore down upon us , and captured us .
Parsing [sent. 1912 len. 50]: This time we did not find any friend in need , and were taken into Table Bay ; for at that time the Cape of Good Hope was in the possession of the Dutch , who , as well as the French , were at war with England . ''
Parsing [sent. 1913 len. 10]: `` How very unfortunate you were , Ready ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1914 len. 4]: said Mrs. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 1915 len. 21]: `` Yes , madam , we were , and I ca n't say much in favour of a Dutch prison .
Parsing [sent. 1916 len. 23]: However , I was very young at that time , and did not care much - I had a light heart . ''
Parsing [sent. 1917 len. 49]: Chapter XXXIV A heavy storm came on soon after they had retired to rest ; the lightning was so vivid that its flashes penetrated through the chinks of the door and windows , and the thunder burst upon them with a noise which prevented them obtaining any sleep .
Parsing [sent. 1918 len. 25]: The children cried and trembled as they lay in the arms of Mrs. Seagrave and Juno , who were almost as much alarmed themselves .
Parsing [sent. 1919 len. 22]: `` This is very awful , '' said Mr. Seagrave to Ready , for they had both risen from their beds .
Parsing [sent. 1920 len. 18]: `` It is indeed , sir ; I never knew a more terrible storm than this . ''
Parsing [sent. 1921 len. 5]: `` Merciful Heaven ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1922 len. 4]: exclaimed Mr. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 1923 len. 75]: As he spoke , they were both thrown back half-stunned ; a crash of thunder burst over the house , which shook everything in it ; a sulphurous smell pervaded the building , and soon afterwards , when they recovered their feet , they perceived that the house was full of smoke , and they heard the wailing of the women and the shrieks of the children in the bed-places on the other side .
Parsing [sent. 1924 len. 8]: `` God have mercy on us ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1925 len. 43]: exclaimed Ready , who was the first to recover himself , and who now attempted to ascertain the injury which had been done : `` the lightning has struck us , and I fear that the house is on fire somewhere . ''
Parsing [sent. 1926 len. 8]: `` My wife - my children ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1927 len. 11]: exclaimed Mr. Seagrave ; `` are they all safe ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1928 len. 6]: `` Yes , yes ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1929 len. 19]: cried Mrs. Seagrave , `` all safe ; Tommy has come to me ; but where is Juno ?
Parsing [sent. 1930 len. 3]: Juno ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1931 len. 4]: Juno answered not .
Parsing [sent. 1932 len. 20]: William darted to the other side of the house , and found Juno lying on her side , motionless .
Parsing [sent. 1933 len. 11]: `` She is dead , father , '' cried William .
Parsing [sent. 1934 len. 34]: `` Help me to carry her out of the house , Mr. Seagrave , '' said Ready , who had lifted up the poor girl ; `` she may be only stunned . ''
Parsing [sent. 1935 len. 22]: They carried Juno out of the house , and laid her on the ground ; the rain poured down in torrents .
Parsing [sent. 1936 len. 36]: Ready left them for a minute , to ascertain if the house was on fire ; he found that it had been in flames at the further corner , but the rain had extinguished it .
Parsing [sent. 1937 len. 15]: He then went back to Mr. Seagrave and William , who were with Juno .
Parsing [sent. 1938 len. 42]: `` I will attend to the girl , sir , '' said Ready ; `` go you and Master William into the house ; Mrs. Seagrave will be too much frightened if she is left alone at such an awful time .
Parsing [sent. 1939 len. 4]: See , sir !
Parsing [sent. 1940 len. 22]: Juno is not dead - her chest heaves - she will come to very soon ; thank God for it ! ''
Parsing [sent. 1941 len. 19]: William and Mr. Seagrave returned to the house ; they found Mrs. Seagrave fainting with anxiety and fear .
Parsing [sent. 1942 len. 20]: The information they brought , that Juno was not killed by the lightning , did much to restore her .
Parsing [sent. 1943 len. 21]: William soothed little Albert , and Tommy in a few minutes was fast asleep again in his father 's arms .
Parsing [sent. 1944 len. 55]: The storm now abated , and as the day began to break , Ready appeared with Juno , who was sufficiently recovered to he able to walk in with his support ; she was put into her bed , and then Ready and Mr. Seagrave went to examine if further mischief had been done .
Parsing [sent. 1945 len. 26]: The lightning had come in at the further end of the house , at the part where the fireplace was intended to have been made .
Parsing [sent. 1946 len. 13]: `` We have been most mercifully preserved , '' said Mr. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 1947 len. 18]: `` Yes , sir , thanks be to God for all his goodness , '' replied Ready .
Parsing [sent. 1948 len. 19]: `` I think we have a large roll of copper wire , Ready ; have we not ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1949 len. 4]: said Mr. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 1950 len. 24]: `` Yes , sir , I was just thinking of it myself ; we will have a lightning-conductor up the first thing . ''
Parsing [sent. 1951 len. 6]: It was now broad daylight .
Parsing [sent. 1952 len. 40]: Mrs. Seagrave dressed herself and the children , and as soon as she was ready , Mr. Seagrave read such portions of the Psalms as were appropriate , and they earnestly joined in a prayer of thankfulness and humility .
Parsing [sent. 1953 len. 26]: William went out to prepare the breakfast , and Ready procured the coil of copper wire from those stores which were stowed under the bed-places .
Parsing [sent. 1954 len. 27]: This he unrolled , and stretched it out straight , and then went for the ladder , which was at the outhouse they had commenced building .
Parsing [sent. 1955 len. 38]: As soon as breakfast was over , Ready and Mr. Seagrave went out again to fix up the lightning-conductor , leaving William to do the work of Juno , who still remained fast asleep in her bed .
Parsing [sent. 1956 len. 46]: `` I think , '' said Ready , `` that one of those two trees which are close together will suit the best ; they are not too near the house , and yet quite near enough for the wire to attract the lightning . ''
Parsing [sent. 1957 len. 17]: `` I agree with you , Ready ; but we must not leave both standing . ''
Parsing [sent. 1958 len. 29]: `` No , sir , but we shall require them both to get up and fix the wire ; after that we will cut down the other . ''
Parsing [sent. 1959 len. 132]: Ready put his ladder against one of the trees , and , taking with him the hammer and a bag of large spike-nails , drove one of the nails into the trunk of the tree till it was deep enough in to bear his weight ; he then drove in another above it , and so he continued to do , standing upon one of them while he drove in another above , till he had reached the top of the tree , close to the boughs ; he then descended , and , leaving the hammer behind him , took up a saw and small axe , and in about ten minutes he had cut off the head of the cocoa-nut tree , which remained a tall , bare pole .
Parsing [sent. 1960 len. 17]: `` Take care , Ready , how you come down , '' said Mr. Seagrave anxiously .
Parsing [sent. 1961 len. 36]: `` Never fear , sir , '' replied Ready ; `` I 'm not so young as I was , but I have been too often at the mast-head , much higher than this . ''
Parsing [sent. 1962 len. 36]: Ready came down again , and then cut down a small pole , to fix with a thick piece of pointed wire at the top of it , on the head of the cocoa-nut tree .
Parsing [sent. 1963 len. 34]: He then went up , lashed the small pole to the head of the tree , made the end of the copper wire fast to the pointed wire , and then he descended .
Parsing [sent. 1964 len. 36]: The other tree near to it was then cut down , and the lower end of the wire buried in the ground at the bottom of the tree on which the lightning-conductor had been fixed .
Parsing [sent. 1965 len. 26]: `` That 's a good job done , sir , '' said Ready , wiping his face , for he was warm with the work .
Parsing [sent. 1966 len. 27]: `` Yes , '' replied Mr. Seagrave ; `` and we must put up another near the outhouse , or we may lose our stores . ''
Parsing [sent. 1967 len. 7]: `` Very true , sir . ''
Parsing [sent. 1968 len. 12]: `` You understand this , William , do n't you ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1969 len. 4]: said his father .
Parsing [sent. 1970 len. 37]: `` O yes , papa ; lightning is attracted by metal , and will now strike the point instead of the house , run down the wire , and only tear up the ground below . ''
Parsing [sent. 1971 len. 32]: `` It 's coming on again , sir , as thick as ever , '' observed the old man ; `` we shall do no work to-day , I 'm afraid .
Parsing [sent. 1972 len. 12]: I 'll just go and see where the stock are . ''
Parsing [sent. 1973 len. 21]: Juno was now up again , and said that she was quite well , with the exception of a headache .
Parsing [sent. 1974 len. 27]: As Ready had predicted , the rain now came on again with great violence , and it was impossible to do any work out of doors .
Parsing [sent. 1975 len. 10]: At the request of William he continued his narrative .
Parsing [sent. 1976 len. 5]: Narrative of Old Ready .
Parsing [sent. 1977 len. 37]: `` Well , William , as soon as they had let go their anchor in Table Bay , we were all ordered on shore , and sent up to a prison close to the Government Gardens .
Parsing [sent. 1978 len. 59]: We were not very carefully watched , as it appeared impossible for us to get away , and I must say we were well treated in every respect ; but we were told that we should be sent to Holland in the first man-of-war which came into the bay , and we did not much like the idea .
Parsing [sent. 1979 len. 49]: `` There were , as I told you , some other boys as well as myself , who belonged to the Indiaman , and we kept very much together , not only because we were more of an age , but because we had been shipmates so long .
Parsing [sent. 1980 len. 73]: Two of these boys , one of whom I have mentioned as Jack Romer , and the other Will Hastings , were my particular friends ; and one day , as we were sitting under the wall warming ourselves , for it was winter time , Romer said , ` How very easy it would be for us to get away , if we only knew where to go to ! ' ''
Parsing [sent. 1981 len. 38]: ` Yes , ' replied Hastings ; ` but where are we to go to , if it is not to the Hottentots and wild savages ; and when we get there , what can we do ?
Parsing [sent. 1982 len. 9]: - we ca n't get any further . '
Parsing [sent. 1983 len. 26]: ` Well , ' said I , ` I would rather be living free among savages , than be shut up in a prison . '
Parsing [sent. 1984 len. 62]: That was our first talk on the subject , but we had many others afterwards ; and as the one or two Dutch soldiers who stood sentry spoke English , and we could talk a little Dutch , we obtained a good deal of information from them ; for they had very often been sent to the frontiers of the colony .
Parsing [sent. 1985 len. 28]: We continued to ask questions , and to talk among ourselves for about two months , and at last we resolved that we would make our escape .
Parsing [sent. 1986 len. 25]: We should have done much better if we had remained where we were ; but there is no putting old heads upon young shoulders .
Parsing [sent. 1987 len. 88]: We saved up our provisions , bought some long Dutch knives , tied our few clothes up in bundles , and one dark night we contrived to remain in the yard without being perceived , when the prisoners were locked up ; and raising a long pole , which lay in the yard , to the top of the wall , with a good deal of scrambling we contrived to get over it , and made off as fast as we could for the Table Mountain . ''
Parsing [sent. 1988 len. 12]: `` What was your reason for going there , Ready ? ''
Parsing [sent. 1989 len. 136]: `` Why , Hastings , who was the oldest , and , I will say , the sharpest of the three , said that we had better stay up there for a few days , till we had made up our minds what to do , and try if we could not procure a musket or two , and ammunition ; for , you see , we had money , as , when the Indiaman was first taken , the captain divided a keg of rupees , which was on board , among the officers and men , in proportion to the wages due to them , thinking it was better for the crew to have the money than to leave it for the Frenchmen ; and we had spent very little while in prison .
Parsing [sent. 1990 len. 70]: There was also another reason why he persuaded us to go to the Table Mountain , which was , that as soon as our escape was found out , they would send parties to look for us ; thinking , of course , that we had made for the interior ; and we should have less chance of being retaken if we travelled after the first search was over .
Parsing [sent. 1991 len. 53]: The soldiers had told us of the lions , and other wild animals , and how dangerous it was to travel , and Hastings said , that not finding us , they would suppose we had been destroyed by the wild beasts , and would not look for us any more . ''
Parsing [sent. 1992 len. 29]: `` Foolish indeed , '' observed Mrs. Seagrave , `` to set off you knew not where , in a country full of wild beasts and savages . ''
Parsing [sent. 1993 len. 10]: `` True enough , madam , '' replied Ready .
Parsing [sent. 1994 len. 54]: `` We ran at first until we were out of breath , and then we walked on as fast as we could - not going right up the mountain , but keeping a slanting direction to the south-west , so as to get away from the town , and more towards False Bay .
Parsing [sent. 1995 len. 33]: `` We had walked about four hours , and began to feel very tired , when the day dawned , and then we looked out for a place to conceal ourselves in .
Parsing [sent. 1996 len. 28]: We soon found a cave with a narrow entrance , large enough inside to hold half-a-dozen of such lads as we were , and we crawled in .
Parsing [sent. 1997 len. 59]: It was quite dry , and , as we were very tired , we lay down with our heads on our bundles , intending to take a nap ; but we had hardly made ourselves comfortable and shut our eyes , when we heard such a screaming and barking that we were frightened out of our lives almost .
Parsing [sent. 1998 len. 9]: We could not think what it could be .
Parsing [sent. 1999 len. 72]: At last Hastings peeped out , and began to laugh ; so Homer and I looked out also , and there we saw about one hundred and fifty large baboons leaping and tumbling about in such a way as I never saw ; they were bigger than we were - indeed , when they stood on their hind legs they were much taller , and they had very large white tusks .
Parsing [sent. 2000 len. 23]: Some of them were females , with young ones on their backs , and they were just as active as the males .
Parsing [sent. 2001 len. 41]: At last they played such antics , that we all burst out into a loud laugh , and we had not ceased when we found the grinning face of one of the largest of those brutes close to our own .
Parsing [sent. 2002 len. 12]: He had dropped from the rock above us , like magic .
Parsing [sent. 2003 len. 27]: We all three backed into the cave , very much frightened , for the teeth of the animal were enormous , and he looked very savage .
Parsing [sent. 2004 len. 24]: He gave a shrill cry , and we perceived all the rest of the herd coming to him as fast as they could .
Parsing [sent. 2005 len. 36]: I said that the cave was large enough to hold six of us ; but there was a sort of inner cave which we had not gone into , as the entrance was much smaller .
Parsing [sent. 2006 len. 72]: Homer cried out , ` Let us go into the inside cave - we can get in one by one ; ' and he backed in ; Hastings followed with his bundle , and I hurried in after him just in time ; for the baboons , who had been chattering to each other for half a minute , came into the outer cave just as I crawled into the inner .
Parsing [sent. 2007 len. 15]: Five or six of them came in , all males , and very large .
Parsing [sent. 2008 len. 47]: The first thing they did was to lay hold of Homer 's bundle , which they soon opened - at once they seized his provisions and rammed them into their pouches , and then they pulled out the other things and tore them all to pieces .
Parsing [sent. 2009 len. 22]: As soon as they had done with the bundle , two of them came towards the inner cave and saw us .
Parsing [sent. 2010 len. 31]: One put his long paw in to seize us ; but Hastings gave him a slash with his knife , and the animal took his paw out again fast enough .
Parsing [sent. 2011 len. 65]: It was laughable to see him hold out his hand to the others , and then taste the blood with the tip of his tongue , and such a chattering I never heard - they were evidently very angry , and more came into the cave and joined them ; then another put in his hand , and received a cut just as before .
Parsing [sent. 2012 len. 30]: At last , two or three at once tried to pull us out , but we beat them all off with our knives , wounding them all very severely .
Parsing [sent. 2013 len. 28]: For about an hour they continued their attempts , and then they went away out of the cave , but remained at the mouth shrieking and howling .
Parsing [sent. 2014 len. 54]: We began to be very tired of this work , and Homer said that he wished he was back in prison again ; and so did I , I can assure you ; but there was no getting out , for had we gone out the animals would have torn us to pieces .
Parsing [sent. 2015 len. 36]: We agreed that we had no chance but the animals becoming tired and going away ; and most anxious we were , for the excitement had made us very thirsty , and we wanted water .
Parsing [sent. 2016 len. 46]: We remained for two hours in this way imprisoned by baboons , when all of a sudden a shrill cry was given by one of the animals , and the whole herd went galloping off as fast as they could , screaming louder than ever .
Parsing [sent. 2017 len. 63]: We waited for a short time to see if they would return , and then Hastings crawled out first , and looking out of the cave very cautiously , said that they were all gone , and that he could see nothing but a Hottentot sitting down watching some cattle ; we therefore all came out , very happy at our release .
Parsing [sent. 2018 len. 24]: That was our first adventure ; we had plenty afterwards ; but I think it is now time we should go to bed .
Parsing [sent. 2019 len. 21]: It is my opinion we shall have a fine day to-morrow , sir ; but there 's no saying . ''
Parsing [sent. 2020 len. 19]: `` I do so want to hear what happened to you afterwards , Ready , '' said William .
Parsing [sent. 2021 len. 47]: `` Well , so you shall ; but there 's a time for everything , and this is bed-time , unless you like to go with me ; the weather has cleared up , and I want to catch a fish or two for to-morrow . ''
Parsing [sent. 2022 len. 23]: Chapter XXXV As Ready had predicted , the weather set in fine after the violent storm of which we have made mention .
Parsing [sent. 2023 len. 65]: For a fortnight , with little intermission , it continued fine , and during that time , Ready , Mr. Seagrave , and William worked from daylight till dusk at the storehouse , which they were so anxious to complete , and were so tired when their work was over , that even William did not ask Ready to go on with his history .
Parsing [sent. 2024 len. 64]: At last the storehouse was complete , thatched and wattled in on three sides , leaving one open for ventilation ; the lower part , which had been arranged for the folding of the stock at night and during the rainy season , was also wattled in with cocoa-nut boughs on three sides , and made a very comfortable retreat for the animals .
Parsing [sent. 2025 len. 29]: The winding path to the storehouse was also cut through the cocoa-nut grove , but the stumps were not removed , as they could not spare the time .
Parsing [sent. 2026 len. 26]: All the stores that they had brought round were put into the storehouse , and they were now ready to take up some other job .
Parsing [sent. 2027 len. 32]: It was , however , agreed that , on the day after the building was finished , they should all have a day 's holiday , which they certainly did require .
Parsing [sent. 2028 len. 28]: William caught some fish , a turtle was speared and wheeled up to the house ; and they not only had a holiday , but a feast .
Parsing [sent. 2029 len. 63]: Mr. Seagrave and William had been walking on the beach with Mrs. Seagrave and the children , while Ready was assisting Juno in cutting up the turtle ; they had shown Mrs. Seagrave the storehouse , and the goats with the four kids had been led there , as there was no longer any occasion for them to remain in the house .
Parsing [sent. 2030 len. 15]: The weather was beautiful , and they agreed to go and examine the garden .
Parsing [sent. 2031 len. 16]: They found that the seeds had not yet commenced sprouting , notwithstanding the heavy rains .
Parsing [sent. 2032 len. 21]: `` I should have thought that so much rain would have made them come up , '' said Mrs. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 2033 len. 44]: `` No , my dear , '' replied Mr. Seagrave ; `` they require more of the sun than they will have till the rainy season is nearly over ; a few days like this , and they will soon be above-ground . ''
Parsing [sent. 2034 len. 19]: `` Let us sit down on this knoll , it is quite dry , '' said Mrs. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 2035 len. 22]: `` I little thought , '' continued she , `` that I could have been so happy in a desert island .
Parsing [sent. 2036 len. 28]: I thought I should feel the loss of books very severely , but I really do not think that I could have found time to read . ''
Parsing [sent. 2037 len. 15]: `` Employment is a source of happiness , especially when you are usefully employed .
Parsing [sent. 2038 len. 39]: An industrious person is always a happy person , provided he is not obliged to work too hard ; and even where you have cause for unhappiness , nothing makes you forget it so soon as occupation . ''
Parsing [sent. 2039 len. 23]: `` But , mamma , we shall not always have so much to do as we have now , '' said William .
Parsing [sent. 2040 len. 24]: `` Of course not , '' replied Mr. Seagrave ; `` and then we shall find our books a great source of enjoyment .
Parsing [sent. 2041 len. 50]: I am anxious to go to the other side of the island , and see what have been spared to us , and whether they have been much damaged ; but that can not be until after the rains are over , and we can use the boat again .
Parsing [sent. 2042 len. 32]: `` Look at this minute insect which is crawling on my finger , '' said Mr. Seagrave , turning to William : `` what a number of legs it has ! ''
Parsing [sent. 2043 len. 13]: `` Yes , I have seen something like it in old books .
Parsing [sent. 2044 len. 17]: How fast it runs with its little legs ; thinner than hairs - how wonderful ! ''
Parsing [sent. 2045 len. 34]: `` Yes , William , '' replied Mr. Seagrave , `` we have only to examine into any portion , however small , of creation , and we are immediately filled with wonder .
Parsing [sent. 2046 len. 37]: There is nothing which points out to us the immensity and the omniscience of the Almighty more than the careful provision which has been made by Him for the smallest and most insignificant of created beings .
Parsing [sent. 2047 len. 27]: This little animal is perhaps one of many millions , who have their term of existence , and their enjoyment , as well as we have .
Parsing [sent. 2048 len. 4]: What is it ?
Parsing [sent. 2049 len. 59]: - an insect of the minutest kind , a nothing in creation ; yet has the same care been bestowed upon its formation : these little legs , hardly visible , have their muscles and their sinews ; and every other portion of its body is as complete , as fearfully and wonderfully fashioned , as our own .
Parsing [sent. 2050 len. 26]: Such is his will ; and what insects we ought to feel ourselves , when compared to the God of power and of love ! ''
Parsing [sent. 2051 len. 29]: `` Let me also point out to you , William , '' continued Mr. Seagrave , `` the infinity of his creative power , displayed in endless variety .
Parsing [sent. 2052 len. 65]: Amongst the millions of men that have been born , and died , if ever yet were there seen two faces or two bodies exactly alike ; nay , if you could examine the leaves upon the trees , although there may be millions upon millions in a forest , you could not discover two leaves of precisely the same form and make . ''
Parsing [sent. 2053 len. 37]: `` I have often tried in vain , '' replied William ; `` yet some animals are so much alike , that I can not perceive any difference between them - sheep , for instance . ''
Parsing [sent. 2054 len. 80]: `` Very true ; you can not tell the difference , because you have not examined them ; but a shepherd , if he has seven hundred sheep under his care , will know every one of them from the others ; which proves that there must be a great difference between them , although not perceptible to the casual observer ; and the same , no doubt , is the case with all other classes of animals . ''
Parsing [sent. 2055 len. 63]: `` Yes , William , '' observed Ready ; `` I have often wondered over the things that I have seen , and I have even in my ignorance felt what your papa has now told you ; and it has brought into my mind the words of Job : ` When I consider , I am afraid of him ' . ''
Parsing [sent. 2056 len. 34]: `` Papa , '' said William , after a pause in the conversation , `` you have referred to the variety - the wonderful variety - shown in the works of the Deity .
Parsing [sent. 2057 len. 10]: Tell me some other prominent feature in creation . ''
Parsing [sent. 2058 len. 13]: `` One of the most remarkable , William , is order . ''
Parsing [sent. 2059 len. 19]: `` Point out to me , papa , where and in what that quality is most observable . ''
Parsing [sent. 2060 len. 109]: `` Everywhere and in everything , my dear boy ; whether we cast our eyes up to the heavens above us , or penetrate into the bowels of the earth , the principle of order is everywhere - everything is governed by fixed laws , which can not be disobeyed : we have order in the seasons , in the tides , in the movement of the heavenly bodies , in the instinct of animals , in the duration of life assigned to each ; from the elephant who lives more than a century , to the ephemeral fly , whose whole existence is limited to an hour .
Parsing [sent. 2061 len. 11]: `` Inanimate nature is subject to the same unvarying laws .
Parsing [sent. 2062 len. 81]: Metals , and rocks , and earths , and all the mineral kingdom follow one law in their crystallization , never varying from the form assigned to them ; each atom depositing itself in the allotted place , until that form is complete : we have order in production , order in decay ; but all is simple to him by whom the planets were thrown out into space , and were commanded to roll in their eternal orbits . ''
Parsing [sent. 2063 len. 26]: `` Yes ; the stars in the heavens are beautiful , '' said William , `` but they are not placed there in order . ''
Parsing [sent. 2064 len. 87]: `` The fixed stars do not appear to us to be in order - that is , they do not stud the heavens at equal distances from each other as we view them ; but you must recollect that they are at very different distances from this earth , spreading over all infinity of space ; and we have reason to suppose that this our earth is but a mere unit in the multitude of created worlds , only one single portion of an infinite whole .
Parsing [sent. 2065 len. 36]: As the stars now appear to us , they are useful to the mariner , enabling him to cross the trackless seas ; and to the astronomer , who calculates the times and seasons . ''
Parsing [sent. 2066 len. 29]: `` What do you mean , papa , by saying that this world of ours is supposed to be but one of a multitude of created worlds ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2067 len. 31]: `` Our little knowledge is bounded to this our own earth , which we have ascertained very satisfactorily to be but one of several planets revolving round our own sun .
Parsing [sent. 2068 len. 55]: I say our own sun , because we have every reason to suppose that each of those fixed stars , and myriads now not visible to the naked eye , are all suns , bright and glorious as our own , and of course throwing light and heat upon unseen planets revolving round them .
Parsing [sent. 2069 len. 21]: Does not this give you some idea of the vastness , the power , and the immensity of God ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2070 len. 16]: `` One almost loses one 's self in the imagination , '' said Mrs. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 2071 len. 104]: `` Yes , '' replied Mr. Seagrave ; `` and it has been surmised by some , who have felt in their hearts the magnificence of the Great Architect , that there must be some point of view in space where all those glorious suns , which seem to us confused in the heavens above us , will appear all symmetrically arranged , will there be viewed in regular order , whirling round in one stupendous and perfect system of beauty and design ; and where can that be , if it is not in that heaven which we hope to gain ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2072 len. 26]: There was a silence for a few moments , when William said , `` They say that there are people who are atheists , papa .

Parsing [sent. 2075 len. 18]: `` No , my child , '' replied Mr. Seagrave ; `` there you are in error .

Parsing [sent. 2078 len. 7]: `` Very true , papa . ''
Parsing [sent. 2079 len. 62]: `` Faith in things seen , if I may use the term , my dear child , may induce men to acknowledge the power and goodness of the Almighty , but it will not make them wise to salvation ; for that end , it is necessary , as the Apostle saith , to have faith in things not seen . Parsing [sent. 2155 len. 10]: Chapter XXXVII The fishpond was commenced the next morning .
Parsing [sent. 2156 len. 60]: Ready , Mr. Seagrave , and William went down together to the beach , and , after much examination , chose a spot about one hundred yards from the turtle-pond as most eligible for the purpose ; the water being shallow , so that at the part farthest from the shore there would not be more than three feet .
Parsing [sent. 2157 len. 78]: `` Now , sir , '' said Ready , `` this is a very simple job ; all we have to do is to collect small rocks and stones , pile them up wall-fashion inside , and with a slope outside , so as to break the force of the waves when the water is a little rough ; of course , the water will find its way through the stones , and will be constantly changed .
Parsing [sent. 2158 len. 76]: It 's very true , that we can at most times catch fish when we want them , but it is not always that we can spare the time , so it 's just as well to have always a certain quantity at hand , to take out at a moment 's warning ; and we can , of course , catch them and put them in here when we have nothing else to do .
Parsing [sent. 2159 len. 27]: Juno will be able to come down and take them out with a spear , when we are away and she wants something for dinner . ''

Parsing [sent. 2164 len. 50]: Ready soon returned with the wheels , and the tub slung with rope on the axle , and by that means they found that they could collect the stones very fast ; Mr. Seagrave and William bringing them , and Ready in the water , building up the wall .
Parsing [sent. 2165 len. 30]: `` We have quite forgot another job which we must put in hand , sir , '' observed Ready ; `` but the fish-pond reminds me of it . ''

Parsing [sent. 2169 len. 21]: When I was at St. Helena , not very long ago , we had a melancholy proof of it . ''

Parsing [sent. 2173 len. 17]: His comrade was very much frightened , and ran to the barracks to tell the story .

Parsing [sent. 2198 len. 8]: After supper , Ready continued his narrative .
Parsing [sent. 2199 len. 52]: `` We remained concealed until it was dark , and then Hastings and Romer , each with a musket on his shoulders and a ham at his back ; and I , being the smallest , with the rifle and the great loaf of bread , set off on our journey .
Parsing [sent. 2200 len. 53]: Our intention was to travel north , as we knew that was the road leading from the colony ; but Hastings had decided that we should first go to the eastward , so as to make what we sailors call a circumbendibus , which would keep us out of the general track .
Parsing [sent. 2201 len. 47]: We passed through the deep sands of False Bay , and after that gradually ascended , getting among brushwood and young trees ; but we saw no signs of cultivation , nor did we pass one house after we had left False Bay astern of us .
Parsing [sent. 2202 len. 33]: About twelve o'clock we were very much fatigued , and longed for a drink of water , but we did not find any , although the moon shone as bright as day .

Parsing [sent. 2209 len. 49]: They were very desolate ; and when night came on we collected brushwood , and cut down branches with our knives , that we might make a fire , not only to warm ourselves , but to scare away the wild beasts , whose howling had already commenced .

Parsing [sent. 2212 len. 11]: We were so tired that we were soon fast asleep .
Parsing [sent. 2213 len. 40]: It had been agreed that Romer should keep the first watch , and Hastings the middle , and I the morning ; but Romer fell asleep , and the consequence was , that the fire was not kept up .

Parsing [sent. 2215 len. 49]: I tried to catch at my musket , but I put out my wrong hand , and laid hold of a still lighted brand out of the fire , which I darted into the animal 's face ; it let me drop directly , and ran away . ''

Parsing [sent. 2221 len. 11]: I was very much frightened , as you may suppose .
Parsing [sent. 2222 len. 20]: As for Romer , he never woke till we pushed him hard , he was so completely knocked up .
Parsing [sent. 2223 len. 30]: This affair , of course , made us more cautious , and afterwards we lighted two fires , and slept between them , one always remaining on the watch .
Parsing [sent. 2224 len. 25]: For a week we travelled on , and as soon as we were over the mountains , we turned our heads to the northward .
Parsing [sent. 2225 len. 47]: Our provisions were all gone , and we were one day without any ; but we killed an antelope called a spring-bock , which gave us provisions for three or four days : there was no want of game after we had descended into the plain .
Parsing [sent. 2226 len. 28]: I forgot to mention , however , a narrow escape we had , just before we had left an extensive forest on the side of the mountain .
Parsing [sent. 2227 len. 33]: We had walked till past noon , and were very much tired ; we decided upon taking our dinner under a large tree , and we threw ourselves down in the shade .
Parsing [sent. 2228 len. 81]: Hastings was lying on his back , with his eyes looking upwards , when he perceived on the lower branch of the tree a panther , which lay along it , his green eyes fixed upon us , and ready to spring ; he seized his musket , and fired it without taking aim , for there was no time ; but the ball entered the stomach of the animal , and , as it appeared , divided its back-bone .
Parsing [sent. 2229 len. 63]: Down came the beast , within three or four feet of where we lay , with a loud roar , and immediately crouched to spring upon Romer ; but it could not , for the back-bone being broken , it had not any power in its hinder quarters , so it raised up its fore quarters , and then dropped down again .
Parsing [sent. 2230 len. 14]: I never saw such rage and fury in an animal in my life .
Parsing [sent. 2231 len. 33]: At first we were too much frightened to fire ; but , perceiving that the beast could not spring , Hastings snatched the musket from Romer and shot it through the head .
Parsing [sent. 2232 len. 18]: `` We were now obliged to hunt for our livelihood , and we became bolder than ever .
Parsing [sent. 2233 len. 39]: Our clothes were all in rags ; but we had plenty of powder and ammunition ; there were hundreds and hundreds of antelopes and gnus in the plain - indeed , sometimes it was impossible to count them .
Parsing [sent. 2234 len. 34]: But this plentiful supply of game was the cause of our being in greater danger , for now , for the first time , we heard the roar of the lions every night .
Parsing [sent. 2235 len. 23]: We made large fires to keep them off , but they often made us tremble when they came near to us . ''
Parsing [sent. 2236 len. 12]: `` Did you ever meet with one in the day-time ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2237 len. 3]: said William .
Parsing [sent. 2238 len. 27]: `` Yes , sir ; we often saw them , but they never attacked us , and we were too much afraid to fire at them .
Parsing [sent. 2239 len. 8]: Once we met one face to face .

Parsing [sent. 2242 len. 12]: We all took to our heels as fast as we could .
Parsing [sent. 2243 len. 31]: I never looked back till I was out of breath : but the lion was content with our running away , and did not take the trouble to follow us .
Parsing [sent. 2244 len. 53]: Well , sir , we had been travelling , we really hardly knew where , but certainly in a northerly direction , for three weeks , and were quite worn out : we now all agreed that we had done a very foolish thing , and would gladly have gone back again .
Parsing [sent. 2245 len. 53]: For my part , I declare that I was willing to lie down and die , if I could have so done , and I became quite indifferent to the roaring of the lions , and felt as if I should be glad if one would have made a meal of me .
Parsing [sent. 2246 len. 15]: At length , one morning , we fell in with a party of natives .

Parsing [sent. 2252 len. 33]: The next day we arrived at a Dutch settlement of three or four farmhouses , called Graaff Reinet ; but I must leave off now , for it is past bed-time . ''
Parsing [sent. 2253 len. 20]: Chapter XXXVIII The construction of the fish-pond proceeded rapidly , and on the third day it was nearly complete .
Parsing [sent. 2254 len. 56]: As soon as all the walls were finished , Ready threw out sand and shingle , so as to make the part next to the beach nearly as deep as the other ; so that there might be sufficient water to prevent the gulls and man-of-war birds from darting down , and striking the fish .
Parsing [sent. 2255 len. 35]: While Ready was thus employed , Mr. Seagrave and William collected more rocks , so as to divide the pond into four parts , at the same time allowing a communication between each part .
Parsing [sent. 2256 len. 43]: These inside walls , as well as the outside , were made of sufficient width to walk upon ; by which means they would have all the fish within reach of the spear , in case they wished to take them out .
Parsing [sent. 2257 len. 11]: The day after the pond was completed the weather changed .
Parsing [sent. 2258 len. 38]: The rain poured down with great force , but it was not accompanied with such terrific thunder and lightning , nor were the storms of so long continuance , as at the commencement of the rainy season .
Parsing [sent. 2259 len. 27]: In the intervals of fine weather they caught a great many fish , which they put into the pond , so that it was well stocked .
Parsing [sent. 2260 len. 40]: But a circumstance occurred , which was the occasion of great alarm to them all ; which was , that one evening William was taken with a shivering , and complained very much of a pain in his head .

Parsing [sent. 2262 len. 17]: He was put into bed , and the next morning he was in a violent fever .
Parsing [sent. 2263 len. 69]: Mr. Seagrave was much alarmed , as the symptoms were worse every hour ; and Ready , who had sat up with him during the night , called Mr. Seagrave out of the house , and said , `` This is a bad case , sir : William was working yesterday with his hat off , and I fear that he has been struck by the sun . ''
Parsing [sent. 2264 len. 23]: The poor boy was for many days in great danger ; and the cheerful house was now one of gloom and silence .
Parsing [sent. 2265 len. 31]: How fervent were now the morning and evening prayers ; how often during the day did his parents offer up a petition to heaven for their dear boy 's recovery .
Parsing [sent. 2266 len. 66]: The weather became finer every day , and it was almost impossible to keep Tommy quiet : Juno went out with him and Albert every morning , and kept them with her while she cooked ; and , fortunately , Vixen had some young ones , and when Juno could no longer amuse them , she brought them two of the puppies to play with .
Parsing [sent. 2267 len. 39]: As for the quiet , meek little Caroline , she would remain during the whole day holding her mother 's hand , and watching her brother , or working with her needle by the side of his bed .
Parsing [sent. 2268 len. 110]: Ready , who could not be idle , had taken the hammer and cold chisel to make the salt-pan , at which he worked during those portions of the day in which his services were not required indoors ; and as he sat chipping away the rock , his thoughts were ever upon William , for he dearly loved the boy for his amiable disposition and his cleverness ; and many a time during the day would he stop his work , and the tears would run down his cheeks as he offered up his petition to the Almighty that the boy might be spared to his afflicted parents .
Parsing [sent. 2269 len. 77]: And those prayers were heard , for on the ninth day William was pronounced by Ready and Mr. Seagrave to have much less fever , and shortly afterwards it left him altogether ; but he was so weak that he could not raise himself in his bed for two or three days ; and it was not till more than a fortnight after the fever had left him that he could go out of the house .
Parsing [sent. 2270 len. 29]: The joy that was expressed by them all when the change took place may be imagined : nor were the thanksgivings less fervent than had been the prayers .

Parsing [sent. 2272 len. 47]: Juno came to their assistance , and was very useful in assisting to drag the wheels which brought the rocks and stones ; and Tommy was also brought down , that he might be out of the way while Mrs. Seagrave and Caroline watched the invalid .
Parsing [sent. 2273 len. 30]: By the time that William was able to go out of the house , the bathing-place was finished , and there was no longer any fear of the sharks .
Parsing [sent. 2274 len. 71]: William came down to the beach with his mother , and looked at the work which had been done ; he was much pleased with it , and said , `` Now , Ready , we have finished everything at home for the present ; all we have to do is to explore the island , and to go to the cove and examine our collection from the wreck . Parsing [sent. 2275 len. 39]: `` Very true , William ; and the weather has been so fine , that I think we may venture upon one or the other in a few days more ; but not till you are stronger . ''
