Parsing [sent. 2289 len. 11]: Nobody but a Hottentot could live upon such flesh . ''
Parsing [sent. 2290 len. 6]: `` What is quagga ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2291 len. 32]: `` A wild ass , partly covered with stripes , but not so much as the zebra ; a pretty animal to look at , but the flesh is very bad .
Parsing [sent. 2292 len. 49]: At last he would give us nothing to eat but quaggas , the same as the Hottentots , while he and his family - for he had a wife and five children - lived upon mutton and the flesh of the antelope , which is very excellent eating .
Parsing [sent. 2293 len. 30]: We asked him to allow us a gun to procure better food , and he kicked Romer so unmercifully , that he could not work for two days afterwards .
Parsing [sent. 2294 len. 28]: Our lives became quite a burden to us ; we were employed all day on the farm , and every day he was more brutal towards us .
Parsing [sent. 2295 len. 20]: At last we agreed that we would stand it no longer , and one evening Hastings told him so .
Parsing [sent. 2296 len. 51]: This put him into a great rage , and he called two of the slaves , and ordered them to tie him to the waggon wheel , swearing that he would cut every bit of skin off his body , and he went into his house to get his whip .
Parsing [sent. 2297 len. 43]: The slaves had hold of Hastings , and were tying him up , for they dared not disobey their master , when he said to us , ` If I am flogged this way , it will be all over with us .
Parsing [sent. 2298 len. 35]: Now 's your time ; run back behind the house , and when he comes out with the whip , do you go in and seize the muskets , which are always ready loaded .

Parsing [sent. 2301 len. 41]: As Romer and I thought this very probable , we did as Hastings told us ; and when the Dutchman had gone towards him where he was tied up , about fifty yards from the house , we went in .

Parsing [sent. 2315 len. 13]: `` How long were you with the farmer at Graaff Reinet ? ''

Parsing [sent. 2318 len. 69]: We were aware that the Dutchmen would shoot us if they came up with us , and that they would come out in strong force against us ; and we were afraid that we had killed the man , and if so , they would hang us as soon as we got to the Cape ; so we were at a great loss to know how to act .

Parsing [sent. 2331 len. 13]: `` I have now to mention a most melancholy event which occurred .

Parsing [sent. 2339 len. 70]: I believe it was very good advice , notwithstanding this unfortunate accident , for we found that when we had travelled all night the lions had more than once followed us the whole time ; and indeed I have often thought since that we were altogether indebted to his mercy who ordereth all things , both in heaven and earth , that we escaped so well as we did .

Parsing [sent. 2352 len. 31]: Chapter XXXIX The next morning , as there was no particular work on hand , Ready and Mr. Seagrave took the lines to add to the stock of the fish-pond .

Parsing [sent. 2374 len. 59]: `` That is difficult to say , William ; but it is supposed that they have become inhabited in much the same way as this our island has been - that is , by people in canoes or boats driven out to sea , and saving their lives by effecting a landing , as we have done . ''
Parsing [sent. 2375 len. 45]: `` I believe that 's the truth , '' replied Ready ; `` I heard say that the Andaman Isles were supposed to have been first inhabited by a slaver full of negroes , who were wrecked on the coast in a typhoon . ''
Parsing [sent. 2376 len. 9]: `` What is a typhoon , Ready ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2377 len. 25]: `` It is much the same as a hurricane , William ; it comes on in India at the change of the monsoons . ''
Parsing [sent. 2378 len. 7]: `` But what are monsoons ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2379 len. 28]: `` Winds that blow regular from one quarter so many months during the year , and then change round and blow from another just as long . ''
Parsing [sent. 2380 len. 21]: `` And what are the trade-winds , which I heard poor Captain Osborn talking about after we left Madeira ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2381 len. 32]: `` The trade-winds blow on the equator , and several degrees north and south of it , from the east to the west , following the course of the sun . ''
Parsing [sent. 2382 len. 11]: `` Is it the sun which produces these winds ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2383 len. 37]: `` Yes , the extreme heat of the sun between the tropics rarefies the air as the earth turns round , and the trade-winds are produced by the rushing in of the less heated air . ''
Parsing [sent. 2384 len. 20]: `` Yes , William ; and the trade-winds produce what they call the Gulf Stream , '' observed Ready .
Parsing [sent. 2385 len. 5]: `` How is that ?
Parsing [sent. 2386 len. 10]: I have heard it spoken of , papa . ''
Parsing [sent. 2387 len. 67]: `` The winds , constantly following the sun across the Atlantic Ocean , and blowing from east to west , have great effect upon the sea , which is forced up into the Gulf of Mexico -LRB- where it is stopped by the shores of America -RRB- , so that it is many feet higher in the Gulf than in the eastern part of the Atlantic .
Parsing [sent. 2388 len. 68]: This accumulation of water must of course find a vent somewhere , and it does in what is called the Gulf Stream , by which the waters are poured out , running very strong to the northward , along the shores of America , and then eastward , passing not far from Newfoundland , until its strength is spent somewhere to the northward of the Azores . ''
Parsing [sent. 2389 len. 53]: `` The Gulf Stream , William , '' said Ready , `` is always several degrees warmer than the sea in general , which is , they say , owing to its waters remaining in the Gulf of Mexico so long , where the heat of the sun is so great . ''
Parsing [sent. 2390 len. 24]: `` What do you mean by the land and sea breezes in the West Indies , and other hot climates , papa ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2391 len. 37]: `` It is the wind first blowing off from the shore , and then blowing from the sea towards the shore , during certain hours of the day , which it does regularly every twenty-four hours .
Parsing [sent. 2392 len. 12]: This is also the effect of the heat of the sun .
Parsing [sent. 2393 len. 28]: The sea breeze commences in the morning , and in the afternoon it dies away , when the land breeze commences , which lasts till midnight . ''
Parsing [sent. 2394 len. 44]: `` There are latitudes close to the trade-winds , '' said Ready , `` where the wind is not certain , where ships have been becalmed for weeks ; the crews have exhausted the water on board , and they have suffered dreadfully .
Parsing [sent. 2395 len. 14]: We call them the Horse latitudes - why , I do not know .
Parsing [sent. 2396 len. 21]: But it is time for us to leave off , and for Master William to go into the house . ''
Parsing [sent. 2397 len. 14]: They returned home , and after supper Ready went on with his narrative .
Parsing [sent. 2398 len. 30]: `` I left off at the time that I was sent on board of the man-of-war , and I was put down on the books as a supernumerary boy .
Parsing [sent. 2399 len. 43]: I was on board of her for nearly four years , and we were sent about from port to port , and from clime to clime , until I grew a strong , tall lad , and was put into the mizen-top .
Parsing [sent. 2400 len. 6]: I found it very comfortable .
Parsing [sent. 2401 len. 69]: I did my duty , and the consequence was , I never was punished ; for a man may serve on board of a man-of-war without fear of being punished , if he only does his duty , and the duty is not very hard either ; not like on board of the merchant vessels , where there are so few hands - there it is hard work .
Parsing [sent. 2402 len. 54]: Of course , there are some captains who command men-of-war who are harsh and severe ; but it was my good fortune to be with a very mild and steady captain , who was very sorry when he was obliged to punish the men , although he would not overlook any improper conduct .
Parsing [sent. 2403 len. 28]: The only thing which was a source of constant unhappiness to me was , that I could not get to England again , and see my mother .
Parsing [sent. 2404 len. 35]: I had written two or three letters , but never had an answer ; and at last I became so impatient that I determined to run away the very first opportunity which might offer .
Parsing [sent. 2405 len. 49]: We were then stationed in the West Indies , and I had very often consultations with Hastings on the subject , for he was quite as anxious to get away as I was ; and we had agreed that we would start off together the very first opportunity .
Parsing [sent. 2406 len. 30]: At last we anchored in Port Royal , Jamaica , and there was a large convoy of West India ships , laden with sugar , about to sail immediately .
Parsing [sent. 2407 len. 41]: We knew that if we could get on board of one , they would secrete us until the time of sailing , for they were short-handed enough , the men-of-war having pressed every man they could lay their hands upon .
Parsing [sent. 2408 len. 41]: There was but one chance , and that was by swimming on board of one of the vessels during the night-time , and that was easy enough , as they were anchored not a hundred yards from our own ship .
Parsing [sent. 2409 len. 17]: What we were afraid of was the sharks , which were so plentiful in the harbour .
Parsing [sent. 2410 len. 37]: However , the night before the convoy was to sail we made up our minds that we would run the risk , for we were so impatient to escape that we did not care for anything .
Parsing [sent. 2411 len. 62]: It was in the middle watch - I recollect it , and shall recollect it all my life , as if it were last night - that we lowered ourselves down very softly from the bows of the ship , and as soon as we were in the water we struck out for one of the West Indiamen close to us .
Parsing [sent. 2412 len. 66]: The sentry at the gangway saw the light in the water made by our swimming through it , and he hailed , of course ; we gave no answer , but swam as fast as we could ; for after he had hailed we heard a bustle , and we knew that the officer of the watch was manning a boat to send after us .
Parsing [sent. 2413 len. 54]: I had just caught hold of the cable of the West Indiaman , and was about to climb up by it , for I was a few yards before Hastings , when I heard a loud shriek , and , turning round , perceived a shark plunging down with Hastings in his jaws .
Parsing [sent. 2414 len. 35]: I was so frightened , that for a short time I could not move : at last I recovered myself , and began to climb up by the cable as fast as I could .
Parsing [sent. 2415 len. 48]: I was just in time , for another shark made a rush at me ; and although I was clear out of the water more than two feet , he sprung up and just caught my shoe by the heel , which he took down with him .
Parsing [sent. 2416 len. 63]: Fear gave me strength , and in a second or two afterwards I was up at the hawse-holes , and the men on board , who had been looking over the bows , and had witnessed poor Hastings ' death , helped me on board , and hurried me down below , for the boat from our ship was now nearly alongside .
Parsing [sent. 2417 len. 36]: When the officer of the boat came on board , they told him they had perceived us both in the water , close to their vessel , and that the sharks had taken us down .
Parsing [sent. 2418 len. 29]: As the shriek of Hastings was heard by the people in the boat , the officer believed that it was the case , and returned to the ship .
Parsing [sent. 2419 len. 61]: I heard the drum beat to quarters on board of the man-of-war , that they might ascertain who were the two men who had attempted to swim away , and a few minutes afterwards they beat the retreat , having put down D. D. against my name on the books , as well as against that of poor Hastings . ''
Parsing [sent. 2420 len. 8]: `` What does D. D. mean ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2421 len. 36]: `` D. stands for discharged from the service ; D. D. stands for dead , '' replied Ready ; `` and it was only through the mercy of Providence that I was not so . ''
Parsing [sent. 2422 len. 13]: `` It was a miraculous escape indeed , '' observed Mr. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 2423 len. 18]: `` Yes , indeed , sir ; I can hardly describe my sensations for some hours afterwards .
Parsing [sent. 2424 len. 14]: I tried to sleep , but could not - I was in agony .
Parsing [sent. 2425 len. 43]: The moment I slumbered , I thought the shark had hold of me , and I would start up and shriek ; and then I said my prayers and tried to go to sleep again , but it was of no use .
Parsing [sent. 2426 len. 42]: The captain of the West Indiaman was afraid that my shrieks would be heard , and he sent me down a tumbler of rum to drink off ; this composed me , and at last I fell into a sound sleep .
Parsing [sent. 2427 len. 43]: When I awoke , I found that the ship was under weigh and with all canvas set , surrounded by more than a hundred other vessels ; the men-of-war who took charge of the convoy , firing guns and making signals incessantly .
Parsing [sent. 2428 len. 14]: It was a glorious sight , and we were bound for Old England .
Parsing [sent. 2429 len. 49]: I felt so happy , that I thought I would risk the jaws of another shark to have regained my liberty , and the chance of being once more on shore in my own country , and able to go to Newcastle and see my poor mother . ''
Parsing [sent. 2430 len. 31]: `` I am afraid that your miraculous escape did you very little good , Ready , '' observed Mrs. Seagrave , `` if you got over it so soon . ''
Parsing [sent. 2431 len. 30]: `` Indeed , madam , it was not so ; that was only the feeling which the first sight of the vessels under weigh for England produced upon me .
Parsing [sent. 2432 len. 14]: I can honestly say that I was a better and more serious person .
Parsing [sent. 2433 len. 56]: The very next night , when I was in my hammock , I prayed very fervently ; and there happened to be a very good old Scotchman on board , the second mate , who talked very seriously to me , and pointed out how wonderful had been my preservation , and I felt it .
Parsing [sent. 2434 len. 28]: It was he who first read the Bible with me , and made me understand it , and , I may say , become fond of it .
Parsing [sent. 2435 len. 23]: I did my duty on our passage home as a seaman before the mast , and the captain was pleased with me .
Parsing [sent. 2436 len. 27]: The ship I was in was bound to Glasgow , and we parted company with the convoy at North Foreland , and arrived safe in port .
Parsing [sent. 2437 len. 41]: The captain took me to the owners , who paid me fifteen guineas for my services during the voyage home ; and as soon as I received the money , I set off for Newcastle as fast as I could .
Parsing [sent. 2438 len. 26]: I had taken a place on the outside of the coach , and I entered into conversation with a gentleman who sat next to me .
Parsing [sent. 2439 len. 25]: I soon found out that he belonged to Newcastle , and I first inquired if Mr. Masterman , the ship-builder , was still alive .
Parsing [sent. 2440 len. 12]: He told me that he had been dead about three months .
Parsing [sent. 2441 len. 11]: ` And to whom did he leave his money ? '
Parsing [sent. 2442 len. 16]: I asked , ` for he was very rich , and had no kin . '
Parsing [sent. 2443 len. 25]: ` He had no relations , ' replied the gentleman , ` and he left all his money to build an hospital and almshouses .
Parsing [sent. 2444 len. 35]: He had a partner in his business latterly , and he left the yard and all the stores to him , I believe , because he did not know whom to leave it to .
Parsing [sent. 2445 len. 44]: There was a lad whom I knew for certain he intended to have adopted and to have made his heir - a lad of the name of Ready ; but he ran away to sea , and has never been heard of since .
Parsing [sent. 2446 len. 18]: It is supposed that he was lost in a prize , for he was traced so far .
Parsing [sent. 2447 len. 6]: Foolish boy that he was .
Parsing [sent. 2448 len. 12]: He might now have been a man of fortune . ' ''
Parsing [sent. 2449 len. 20]: ` Very foolish indeed , ' replied I. '' ` Yes ; but he has harmed more than himself .
Parsing [sent. 2450 len. 41]: His poor mother , who doted upon him , as soon as she heard that he was lost , pined away by degrees , and -- ' '' ` You do n't mean to say that she is dead ? '
Parsing [sent. 2451 len. 11]: interrupted I , seizing the gentleman by the arm . ''
Parsing [sent. 2452 len. 24]: ` Yes , ' replied he , looking at me with surprise ; ` she died last year of a broken heart . '

Parsing [sent. 2456 len. 24]: `` Thank you , sir , it will be better ; for I feel my old eyes dim with tears , even now .
Parsing [sent. 2457 len. 43]: It 's a dreadful thing in after-life to reflect upon , that your foolish conduct has hastened the death of a most kind mother ; but so it was , William , and I give you the truth for your advantage . ''
Parsing [sent. 2458 len. 27]: Chapter XL A few mornings afterwards , Juno came in before breakfast with six eggs in her apron , which she had found in the hen-house .
Parsing [sent. 2459 len. 33]: `` Look , Missy Seagrave - fowls lay eggs - soon have plenty - plenty for Master William - make him well again - and plenty for chickens by and by . ''
Parsing [sent. 2460 len. 18]: `` You have n't taken them all out of the nests , Juno ; have you ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2461 len. 15]: `` No ; leave one in each nest for hen to see . '' ''

Parsing [sent. 2463 len. 33]: `` I am getting quite strong now , mother , '' replied William ; `` I think it would be better to leave the eggs for the hens to sit upon . ''
Parsing [sent. 2464 len. 19]: `` No , no , William ; your health is of more consequence than having early chickens . ''

Parsing [sent. 2467 len. 31]: On the fourth day the hens appeared also not to have laid , much to the surprise of Mrs. Seagrave ; as when hens commence laying eggs they usually continue .
Parsing [sent. 2468 len. 28]: On the fifth morning , when they sat down to breakfast , Master Tommy did not make his appearance , and Mrs. Seagrave asked where he was .
Parsing [sent. 2469 len. 29]: `` I suspect , madam , '' said Old Ready , laughing , `` that Tommy will not come either to his breakfast or his dinner to-day . ''
Parsing [sent. 2470 len. 9]: `` What can you mean , Ready ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2471 len. 4]: said Mrs. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 2472 len. 10]: `` Why , madam , I will tell you .

Parsing [sent. 2474 len. 53]: I could not find any eggs , but I found the egg-shells , hid under some cocoa-nut leaves ; and I argued , that if an animal , supposing there was any on the island , had taken the eggs , it would not have been so careful to hide the egg-shells .
Parsing [sent. 2475 len. 58]: So , this morning , I fastened up the door of the hen-house , and only left open the little sliding door , by which the fowls go in to roost ; and then , after you were up , I watched behind the trees , and saw Tommy come out , and go to the hen-house .
Parsing [sent. 2476 len. 19]: He tried the door , and finding it fast , crept into the hen-house by the little sliding-door .
Parsing [sent. 2477 len. 31]: As soon as he was in I let down the slide , and fastened it with a nail ; so there he is , caught in his own trap . ''
Parsing [sent. 2478 len. 14]: `` And there shall he remain all day , the little glutton ! ''
Parsing [sent. 2479 len. 4]: said Mr. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 2480 len. 23]: `` Yes , it will serve him right , '' replied Mrs. Seagrave ; `` and be a lesson to him . ''

Parsing [sent. 2482 len. 28]: Tommy remained very quiet for an hour , when he commenced roaring ; but it was of no use , no one paid any attention to him .
Parsing [sent. 2483 len. 36]: At dinner-time he began to roar again , but with as little success : it was not till the evening that the door of the hen-house was opened , and Tommy permitted to come out .

Parsing [sent. 2489 len. 7]: After which Ready continued his narrative .

Parsing [sent. 2491 len. 22]: I was in agony until I arrived at Newcastle , where I could ascertain all the facts connected with her decease .

Parsing [sent. 2493 len. 17]: ` Yes , sir , ' replied I , very sorrowfully , ` I am . '

Parsing [sent. 2495 len. 30]: It was not your going to sea , but the report of your death , which preyed so much upon her mind ; and that was not your fault .
Parsing [sent. 2496 len. 17]: You must come with me , as I have something to say to you . ' ''

Parsing [sent. 2498 len. 27]: It is very true that I did not intend to distress my mother ; and that the report of my death was no fault of mine .
Parsing [sent. 2499 len. 25]: But I can not help feeling that , if I had not been so thoughtless , she would be still alive and happy . '
Parsing [sent. 2500 len. 17]: The gentleman gave me his address , and I promised to call upon him next morning .
Parsing [sent. 2501 len. 13]: I then went to the house my mother used to live in .
Parsing [sent. 2502 len. 21]: I knew that she was not there ; yet I was disappointed and annoyed when I heard merry laughter within .
Parsing [sent. 2503 len. 55]: I looked in , for the door was open ; in the corner where my mother used to sit , there was a mangle , and two women busily at work ; others were ironing at a large table ; and when they cried out to me , ` What do you want ? '
Parsing [sent. 2504 len. 30]: and laughed at me , I turned away in disgust , and went to a neighbouring cottage , the inmates of which had been very intimate with my mother .
Parsing [sent. 2505 len. 22]: I found the wife at home , but she did not know me ; and I told her who I was .
Parsing [sent. 2506 len. 26]: She had attended my mother during her illness , till the day of her death ; and she told me all I wished to know .
Parsing [sent. 2507 len. 55]: It was some little relief to my mind to hear that my poor mother could not have lived , as she had an incurable cancer ; but at the same time the woman told me that I was ever in her thoughts , and that my name was the last word on her lips .
Parsing [sent. 2508 len. 21]: She also said that Mr. Masterman had been very kind to my mother , and that she had wanted nothing .
Parsing [sent. 2509 len. 14]: I then asked her to show me where my mother had been buried .
Parsing [sent. 2510 len. 24]: She put on her bonnet , and led me to the grave , and then , at my request , she left me .
Parsing [sent. 2511 len. 27]: I seated myself down by the mound of turf which covered her , and long and bitterly did I weep her loss and pray for forgiveness .
Parsing [sent. 2512 len. 26]: `` It was quite dark when I left the spot and went back to the cottage of the kind woman who had attended my mother .
Parsing [sent. 2513 len. 27]: I conversed with her and her husband till late , and then , as they offered me a bed , I remained with them that night .
Parsing [sent. 2514 len. 34]: Next morning I went to keep my appointment with the gentleman whom I had met in the coach : I found by the brass plate on the door that he was a lawyer .
Parsing [sent. 2515 len. 110]: He desired me to sit down , and then he closed the door carefully , and having asked me many questions , to ascertain if I was really Masterman Ready , he said he was the person employed at Mr. Masterman 's death , and that he had found a paper which was of great consequence , as it proved that the insurance of the vessel which had belonged to my father and Mr. Masterman , and which had been lost , had not been made on Mr. Masterman 's share only , but upon my father 's as well , and that Mr. Masterman had defrauded my mother .
Parsing [sent. 2516 len. 104]: He said he had found the paper in a secret drawer some time after Mr. Masterman 's death , and that my mother being dead , and I being supposed to be dead , he did not see any use in making known so disagreeable a circumstance ; but that , now I had re-appeared , it was his duty so to do , and that he would arrange the matter for me , if I pleased , with the corporation of the town , to whom all Mr. Masterman 's property had been left in trust to build an hospital and almshouses .
Parsing [sent. 2517 len. 49]: He said that the insurance on the vessel was three thousand pounds , and that one-third of the vessel belonged to my father , so that a thousand pounds were due to him , which the interest for so many years would increase to above two thousand pounds .
Parsing [sent. 2518 len. 19]: This was good news for me , and you may suppose I readily agreed to all he proposed .
Parsing [sent. 2519 len. 47]: He set to work at once , and having called together the mayor and corporation of the town , and proved the document , they immediately agreed that I was entitled to the money , and that it should be paid to me without any contest .
Parsing [sent. 2520 len. 17]: Thus you see , Master William , was a new temptation thrown in my way . ''
Parsing [sent. 2521 len. 8]: `` How do you mean a temptation ?
Parsing [sent. 2522 len. 12]: It surely was very fortunate , Ready , '' said William .
Parsing [sent. 2523 len. 66]: `` Yes , William , it was , as people say , fortunate , according to the ideas of the world ; every one congratulated me , and I was myself so inflated with my good fortune , that I forgot all the promises of amendment , all the vows of leading a good life , which I made over my poor mother 's grave .
Parsing [sent. 2524 len. 15]: Now do you perceive why I called it a temptation , Master William ? ''
Parsing [sent. 2525 len. 41]: `` My dear child , '' said Mr. Seagrave , `` riches and prosperity in this world prove often the greatest of temptations ; it is adversity that chastens and amends us , and which draws us to God . ''
Parsing [sent. 2526 len. 29]: `` As soon as the money was in my own hands , '' continued Ready , `` I began to squander it away in all manner of folly .
Parsing [sent. 2527 len. 26]: Fortunately , I had not received it more than ten days , when the Scotch second mate came like a guardian angel to save me .
Parsing [sent. 2528 len. 55]: As soon as I had made known to him what had taken place , he reasoned with me , pointed out to me that I had an opportunity of establishing myself for life , and proposed that I should purchase a part of a vessel , on condition that I was captain of her .
Parsing [sent. 2529 len. 63]: I liked this idea very much , and being convinced that I had been making a fool of myself , I resolved to take his advice ; but one thing only restrained me : I was still very young , not more than twenty years old ; and although I could navigate at one time , I had latterly paid no attention .
Parsing [sent. 2530 len. 51]: I told Sanders this , and he replied , that if I would take him as my first mate , that difficulty would be got over , as he could navigate well , and that I could learn to do so in the first voyage ; so all was arranged .
Parsing [sent. 2531 len. 15]: `` Fortunately , I had not spent above one hundred pounds of the money .

Parsing [sent. 2533 len. 34]: At last , he found that there was one ready for launching , which , in consequence of the failure of the house for which it was built , was to be sold .
Parsing [sent. 2534 len. 43]: He made inquiries , and having found who was likely to purchase her - that it was a very safe and respectable firm - he made a proposal for me that I should take one-fourth share of her , and command her .
Parsing [sent. 2535 len. 33]: As Sanders was very respectable , and well known to be a steady man , his recommendation was attended to so far that the parties wished to see and speak to me .
Parsing [sent. 2536 len. 17]: They were satisfied with me , young as I was , and the bargain was made .
Parsing [sent. 2537 len. 39]: I paid down my two thousand pounds for my share , and as soon as the vessel was launched , was very busy with Sanders , whom I had chosen as first mate , in fitting her out .
Parsing [sent. 2538 len. 27]: The house which had purchased her with me was a West India firm , and the ship was of course intended for the West India trade .
Parsing [sent. 2539 len. 100]: I had two or three hundred pounds left , after I had paid my share of the vessel , and this I employed in purchasing a venture on my own account , and providing nautical instruments , & c. I also fitted myself out , for you see , William , although Sanders had persuaded me to be rational , I was still puffed up with pride at the idea of being captain of my own ship ; it was too great a rise for one who had just before been a lad in the mizen-top of a man-of-war .
Parsing [sent. 2540 len. 16]: I dressed myself very smart - wore white shirts , and rings on my fingers .
Parsing [sent. 2541 len. 33]: Indeed , as captain and part owner of a fine vessel , I was considered as somebody , and was often invited to the table of the other owners of the vessel .
Parsing [sent. 2542 len. 33]: I was well off , for my pay was ten pounds a month , independent of what my own venture might produce , and my quarter-share of the profits of the vessel .
Parsing [sent. 2543 len. 46]: This may be considered as the most prosperous portion of my life ; and so , if you please , we will leave off here for to-night , for I may as well tell you at once that it did not last very long . ''
Parsing [sent. 2544 len. 61]: Chapter XLI For several days after , they were employed in clearing away the stumps of the cocoa-nut trees in the winding path to the storehouse ; and as soon as that work was finished , Ready put up a lightning-conductor at the side of the storehouse , like the one which he had put up near to the cottage .
Parsing [sent. 2545 len. 19]: They had now got through all the work that they had arranged to do during the rainy season .
Parsing [sent. 2546 len. 20]: The ewes had lambed , but both the sheep and the goats began to suffer for want of pasture .
Parsing [sent. 2547 len. 29]: For a week they had no rain , and the sun burst out very powerfully ; and Ready was of opinion that the rainy season was now over .
Parsing [sent. 2548 len. 22]: William had become quite strong again , and he was very impatient that they should commence the survey of the island .
Parsing [sent. 2549 len. 36]: After a great deal of consultation , it was at last settled , that Ready and William should make the first survey to the southward , and then return and report what they had discovered .
Parsing [sent. 2550 len. 19]: This was decided upon on the Saturday evening , and on the Monday morning they were to start .
Parsing [sent. 2551 len. 20]: The knapsacks were got ready , and well filled with boiled salt pork , and flat cakes of bread .
Parsing [sent. 2552 len. 31]: They were each to have a musket and ammunition , and a blanket was folded up to carry on the shoulders , that they might sleep on it at night .
Parsing [sent. 2553 len. 25]: Ready did not forget his compass , or the small axes , for them to blaze the trees as they went through the wood .
Parsing [sent. 2554 len. 11]: The whole of Saturday was occupied in making their preparations .
Parsing [sent. 2555 len. 29]: After supper , Ready said , `` Now , William , before we start on our travels , I think I may as well wind up my history .
Parsing [sent. 2556 len. 42]: I have n't a great deal more to tell , as my good fortune did not last long ; and after my remaining so long in a French prison , my life was one continued chapter of from bad to worse .
Parsing [sent. 2557 len. 14]: Our ship was soon ready , and we sailed with convoy for Barbadoes .
Parsing [sent. 2558 len. 34]: Sanders proved a good navigator , and from him , before we arrived at Barbadoes , I gained all the knowledge which I required to enable me to command and navigate my vessel .
Parsing [sent. 2559 len. 42]: Sanders attempted to renew our serious conversation , but my property had made me vain ; and now that I felt I could do without his assistance , I not only kept him at a distance , but assumed the superior .
Parsing [sent. 2560 len. 23]: This was a very ungrateful return for his kindness to me ; but it is too often the case in this world .
Parsing [sent. 2561 len. 26]: Sanders was very much annoyed , and on our arrival at Barbadoes , he told me that it was his intention to quit the vessel .
Parsing [sent. 2562 len. 35]: I replied very haughtily , that he might do as he pleased ; the fact is , I was anxious to get rid of him , merely because I was under obligations to him .
Parsing [sent. 2563 len. 17]: Well , sir , Sanders left me , and I felt quite happy at his departure .
Parsing [sent. 2564 len. 23]: My ship was soon with a full cargo of sugar on board of her , and we waited for convoy to England .
Parsing [sent. 2565 len. 30]: When at Barbadoes , I had an opportunity to buy four brass guns , which I mounted on deck , and had a good supply of ammunition on board .
Parsing [sent. 2566 len. 59]: I was very proud of my vessel , as she had proved in the voyage out to be a very fast sailer : indeed , she sailed better than some of the men-of-war which convoyed us ; and now that I had guns on board , I considered myself quite safe from any of the enemies ' privateers .
Parsing [sent. 2567 len. 43]: While we were waiting for convoy , which was not expected for a fortnight , it blew a very heavy gale , and my ship , as well as others , dragged their anchors , and were driven out of Carlisle Bay .
Parsing [sent. 2568 len. 19]: We were obliged to make sail to beat into the bay again , it still blowing very fresh .
Parsing [sent. 2569 len. 66]: What with being tired waiting so long for convoy , and the knowledge that arriving before the other West Indiamen would be very advantageous , I made up my mind that , instead of beating up into the bay again , I would run for England without protection , trusting to the fast sailing of my vessel and the guns which I had on board .
Parsing [sent. 2570 len. 38]: I forgot at the time that the insurance on the vessel was made in England as ` sailing with convoy ' , and that my sailing without would render the insurance void , if any misfortune occurred .
Parsing [sent. 2571 len. 19]: Well , sir , I made sail for England , and for three weeks everything went on well .
Parsing [sent. 2572 len. 53]: We saw very few vessels , and those which did chase us could not come up with us ; but as we were running with a fair wind up channel , and I had made sure of being in port before night , a French privateer hove in sight and gave chase .
Parsing [sent. 2573 len. 21]: We were obliged to haul our wind , and it blowing very fast , we carried away our main-top mast .
Parsing [sent. 2574 len. 53]: This accident was fatal ; the privateer came alongside of us and laid us by the board , and that night I was in a French prison , and , I may say , a pauper ; for the insurance of the vessel was void , from my having sailed without convoy .
Parsing [sent. 2575 len. 38]: I felt that I had no one to thank but myself for the unfortunate position I was in ; at all events , I was severely punished , for I remained a prisoner for nearly six years .
Parsing [sent. 2576 len. 39]: I contrived to escape with three or four others ; we suffered dreadfully , and at last arrived in England , in a Swedish vessel , without money , or even clothes that would keep out the weather .
Parsing [sent. 2577 len. 58]: Of course , I had nothing to do but to look out for a berth on board of a ship , and I tried for that of second mate , but without success ; I was too ragged and looked too miserable ; so I determined , as I was starving , to go before the mast .

Parsing [sent. 2583 len. 26]: But if he would not remember my conduct , I could not forget it , and I told him so , and begged his forgiveness .
Parsing [sent. 2584 len. 18]: Well , sir , that good man , as long as he lived , was my friend .

Parsing [sent. 2588 len. 72]: Since that , I have always been as a common seaman on board of different vessels ; but I have been well treated and respected , and I may add , I have not been unhappy , for I felt that property would have only led me into follies , and have made me forget , that in this world we are to live so as to prepare ourselves for another .

Parsing [sent. 2755 len. 142]: As soon as they had returned to the house , a consultation was held as to their future proceedings ; and , after some debate , it was agreed that it would be advisable that they should take the boat out of the sand ; and , as soon as it was ready , examine the reef on the southward , to see if they could find a passage through it , as it would take a long while to go round it ; and , as soon as that was accomplished , Mr. Seagrave , Ready , William , and Juno should all go through the wood , carrying with them a tent to pitch on the newly-discovered piece of ground : and that they should set up a flag-staff at the little harbour , to point out its position .

Parsing [sent. 2765 len. 46]: Chapter XLIII As usual , Ready was the first up on the following morning , and having greeted Juno , who followed him out of the house , he set off on his accustomed rounds , to examine into the stock and their other possessions .
Parsing [sent. 2766 len. 10]: He was standing in the garden at the point .
Parsing [sent. 2767 len. 91]: First he thought that it would be necessary to get ready some sticks for the peas , which were now seven or eight inches out of the ground ; he had proceeded a little farther , to where the calivances , or French haricot beans , had been sown , and had decided upon the propriety of hoeing up the earth round them , as they were a very valuable article of food , that would keep , and afford many a good dish during the rainy or winter season .
Parsing [sent. 2768 len. 26]: He had gone on to ascertain if the cucumber seeds had shown themselves above-ground , and was pleased to find that they were doing well .
Parsing [sent. 2769 len. 65]: He said to himself , `` We have no vinegar , that I know of , but we can preserve them in salt and water , as they do in Russia ; it will be a change , at all events ; '' and then he raised his eyes and looked out to the offing , and , as usual , scanned the horizon .
Parsing [sent. 2770 len. 19]: He thought he saw a ship to the north-east , and he applied his telescope to his eye .
Parsing [sent. 2771 len. 10]: He was not mistaken - it was a vessel .
Parsing [sent. 2772 len. 33]: The old man 's heart beat quick ; he dropped his telescope on his arm , and fetched some heavy breaths before he could recover from the effect of this unexpected sight .
Parsing [sent. 2773 len. 35]: After a minute , he again put his telescope to his eye , and then made her out to be a brig , under top-sails and top-gallant sails , steering directly for the island .
Parsing [sent. 2774 len. 18]: Ready walked to the rocky point , from which they fished , and sat down to reflect .
Parsing [sent. 2775 len. 24]: Could it be that the vessel had been sent after them , or that she had by mere chance come among the islands ?
Parsing [sent. 2776 len. 30]: He decided after a short time that it must be chance , for none could know that they were saved , much less that they were on the island .
Parsing [sent. 2777 len. 28]: Her steering towards the island must then be either that she required water or something else ; perhaps she would alter her course and pass by them .
Parsing [sent. 2778 len. 41]: `` At all events , '' thought the old man , `` we are in the hands of God , who will , at his own time and in his own way , do with us as he thinks fit .
Parsing [sent. 2779 len. 13]: I will not at present say anything to Mr. and Mrs. Seagrave .
Parsing [sent. 2780 len. 13]: It would be cruel to raise hopes which might end in disappointment .
Parsing [sent. 2781 len. 6]: A few hours will decide .
Parsing [sent. 2782 len. 15]: And yet I can not do without help - I must trust William . ''
Parsing [sent. 2783 len. 17]: Ready rose , examined the vessel with his telescope , and then walked towards the house .
Parsing [sent. 2784 len. 13]: William was up , and the remainder of the family were stirring .

Parsing [sent. 2786 len. 9]: A few hours will decide the question . ''
Parsing [sent. 2787 len. 6]: William readily gave his promise .
Parsing [sent. 2788 len. 26]: `` There is a vessel off the island ; she may be the means of rescuing us , or she may pass without seeing us .
Parsing [sent. 2789 len. 21]: It would be too cruel a disappointment to your father and mother , if the latter were the case . ''

Parsing [sent. 2791 len. 10]: `` Oh , Ready , how grateful I am !

Parsing [sent. 2801 len. 11]: William and Ready went to the storehouse for the axe .

Parsing [sent. 2822 len. 6]: Ready 's stratagem answered well .

Parsing [sent. 3550 len. 17]: Now , these three animals are very much maligned , for they are all sagacious animals .
Parsing [sent. 3551 len. 50]: But the fact is that , as regards the ass , we have only very sorry specimens of the animal in England ; they are stunted and small , and , from want of corn and proper food , besides being very ill-treated , are slow and dull-looking animals .
Parsing [sent. 3552 len. 106]: The climate of England is much too cold for the ass ; in the south of France and the Mediterranean , where it is much warmer , the ass is a much finer animal ; but to see it in perfection we must go to the Torrid Zone in Guinea , right on the equator , the hottest portion of the globe , where the ass , in its native state and in its native country , is a handsome creature and as fleet as the wind ; indeed , supposed to be , and mentioned in the Scriptures as the fleetest animal in creation .
Parsing [sent. 3553 len. 27]: The fact is , that in Asia , especially in Palestine and Syria , asses were in great repute , and used in preference to horses .

Parsing [sent. 3564 len. 26]: It is a remarkable instance of a graminivorous or grass-eating animal being changed for a time into a flesh-eating , or rather into fish-eating animal .
Parsing [sent. 3565 len. 25]: But there are other animals which can live under any temperature , as the wolf , the fox , the hare , and rabbit .
Parsing [sent. 3566 len. 47]: It is a curious provision , - that the sheep and goats in the hottest climates throw off their warm covering of wool , and retain little better than hair ; while , removed to a cold climate , they recover their warm covering immediately . ''
Parsing [sent. 3567 len. 11]: `` But a goat has no wool , papa . ''
Parsing [sent. 3568 len. 11]: `` What are Cashmere shawls made of , William ? ''
Parsing [sent. 3569 len. 7]: `` Very true , papa . ''
Parsing [sent. 3570 len. 22]: `` Most animals have a certain increase of covering as they recede further from the warm climates to the cold ones .
Parsing [sent. 3571 len. 22]: Wolves and foxes , hares and rabbits , change the colour of their skins to white when they get far north .
Parsing [sent. 3572 len. 25]: The little English stoat , which is destroyed by the gamekeepers , becomes the beautiful snow-white ermine in Russia and other cold countries . ''
Parsing [sent. 3573 len. 94]: `` Well , papa , I think it a great advantage to man , and a proof of the Almighty 's care of him and kindness to him , in permitting all the animals most useful to him to be able to live in any country ; but I do n't know whether I am wrong in saying so , papa : I can not see why an animal like the wolf should not have been kept to his own climate , like the lion and tiger , and other ferocious animals . ''
Parsing [sent. 3574 len. 32]: `` You have started a question , William , which I am glad you have done , rather than it should have remained on your mind , and have puzzled you .
Parsing [sent. 3575 len. 43]: It is true that the shepherd might agree with you , that the wolf is a nuisance ; equally true that the husband man may exclaim , What is the good of thistles , and the various weeds which choke the soil ?
Parsing [sent. 3576 len. 35]: But , my dear boy , if they are not , which I think they are , for the benefit of man , at all events they are his doom for the first transgression .
Parsing [sent. 3577 len. 63]: ` Cursed is the ground for thy sake - thorns and thistles shall it bring forth to thee - and by the sweat of thy brow shalt thou eat bread , ' was the Almighty 's sentence ; and it is only by labour that the husbandman can obtain his crops , and by watchfulness that the shepherd can guard his flocks .
Parsing [sent. 3578 len. 25]: Labour is in itself a benefit : without exercise there would be no health , and without health there would be no enjoyment . ''
Parsing [sent. 3579 len. 7]: `` I see now , papa .
Parsing [sent. 3580 len. 23]: You have mentioned the animals which can live in all climates ; will you not tell us something about other animals ? ''
Parsing [sent. 3581 len. 66]: `` There is but one remark to make , William , which is , that animals indigenous to , that is , originally to be found in , any one portion of the globe , invariably are so fashioned as to be most fit for that country , and have the food also most proper for them growing or to be obtained in that country .
Parsing [sent. 3582 len. 36]: Take , for instance , the camel , an animal fashioned expressly for the country to which he is indigenous , and without whose aid all communication must have been stopped between Asia and Africa .
Parsing [sent. 3583 len. 22]: He is called the ` Ship of the Desert ' ; for the desert is a ` sea of sand ' .
Parsing [sent. 3584 len. 57]: His feet are so fashioned that he can traverse the sands with facility ; he can live upon the coarsest vegetable food and salt plants which are found there , and he has the capacity of carrying water in a sort of secondary stomach , for his own supply where no water is to be found .
Parsing [sent. 3585 len. 39]: Here is an animal wonderfully made by the Almighty for an express locality , and for the convenience of man in that country ; for , in England , or elsewhere , he would be of no value .
Parsing [sent. 3586 len. 26]: But it is late , my dear William ; so we will first thank him for all his mercies , and then to bed . ''
Parsing [sent. 3587 len. 20]: Chapter LVII The next morning was one of bustle ; there was packing up and every preparation for departure .

Parsing [sent. 3608 len. 44]: After a little consultation with Mr. Seagrave , Ready marked out a square of cocoa-nut trees surrounding the storehouse , so as to leave a space within them of about twenty yards each side , which they considered large enough for the inclosure .
Parsing [sent. 3609 len. 54]: These cocoa-nut trees were to serve as the posts between which were to be fixed other cocoa-nut trees cut down , and about fourteen feet high , so as to form a palisade or stockade , which could not be climbed over , and would protect them from any attack of the savages .
Parsing [sent. 3610 len. 45]: As soon as the line of trees had been marked out , they set to work cutting down all the trees within the line , and then outside to a distance of ten yards , so as to give them room for their work .
Parsing [sent. 3611 len. 46]: Ready cut out cross-pieces , to nail from tree to tree , and now they found the advantage of having saved so many of the large spike nails , without which they never could have made so good or so quick a job of it .
Parsing [sent. 3612 len. 30]: Mr. Seagrave cut down trees , William and Juno sawed them off at a proper length with one of the cross-cut saws , and then carried them to Ready .
Parsing [sent. 3613 len. 48]: They soon had more cut out than be could use , and then they dragged away the tops and branches , and piled them at a distance on the ground , to use as winter fuel , while Mr. Seagrave helped Ready in fixing up the palisades .
Parsing [sent. 3614 len. 16]: They worked very hard that day , and were not sorry to go to bed .
Parsing [sent. 3615 len. 12]: Ready , however , took an opportunity to speak to William .
Parsing [sent. 3616 len. 32]: `` I think , '' said he , `` that now we are here again , it will be necessary to keep a sort of night-watch , in case of accident .
Parsing [sent. 3617 len. 33]: I shall not go to bed till it is quite dark , which it will be by nine o'clock , and shall have my glass to examine the offing the last thing .
Parsing [sent. 3618 len. 143]: You see , there is little fear of the savages coming here in the night-time , but they may just before night or very early in the morning , so one of us must be up again before daybreak , that is between two and three o'clock in the morning , to see if there is anything to be seen of them ; if there is not , of course we may go to bed again , as they can not arrive till many hours afterwards ; and we must watch the wind and weather , if it is favourable for them to come to us , which , indeed , the wind will not be except at the commencement of the rainy season but it may be very light , and then they would not care for its being against them .
Parsing [sent. 3619 len. 99]: I 've been thinking of it , William , a great deal , and my idea is , that it will be at the beginning of the rainy season that we shall have a visit , if we have one at all ; for you see that the wind do n't blow regular from one quarter , as it does now , but is variable , and then they can make sail in their canoes , and come here easily , instead of pulling between thirty and forty miles , which is hard work against wind and current .
Parsing [sent. 3620 len. 17]: Still , we must not be careless and we must keep a good look-out even now .
Parsing [sent. 3621 len. 35]: I do n't want to fret your father and Mrs. Seagrave with my fears on the subject , but I tell you what I really think , and what we ought to do . ''
Parsing [sent. 3622 len. 33]: `` I agree with you , Ready , and I will take care to be up before daybreak , and examine very carefully with the spy-glass as soon as the day dawns .
Parsing [sent. 3623 len. 18]: You take the night part , and I will do the morning part of the watching . ''

Parsing [sent. 3700 len. 74]: The stockade was now almost finished ; the door was the occasion of a good deal of consultation ; at last , it was agreed that it would be better to have a door of stout oak plank , but with second door-posts inside , about a foot apart from the door , between which could be inserted short poles one above the other , so as to barricade it within when required .

Parsing [sent. 3758 len. 22]: Indeed , he was so active , that Mrs. Seagrave praised him before his papa , and Tommy was quite proud .

Parsing [sent. 3777 len. 13]: `` Very well , I am always glad of your company . ''

Parsing [sent. 3945 len. 35]: Chapter LXIII But the moaning of the children was very soon after dusk drowned by the yells of the savages , who , as Ready had prognosticated , now advanced to the night attack .
